O children, now that I have completed this book for you, which is the last of the science of medicine and from whose completion through its expositions and declarations I have seen that you will reach the end of it, and by means of which, it is to me clear that you will reach it through this treatise, which is a part of the action with the hand, that is surgery.
For the action with the hand is now prohibited in our realm and in our times deprived of it, while greatly threatening the science of medicine; and its practices are obscured and only a few small descriptions remain of them in the books of the ancients, which they have altered and introduced into errors and delusions, closing their minds to it, lengthening their words, and losing the art.
And it has seemed good to me that you animate this through the arrangement of this treatise according to the way of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and so that it may come with the forms of the instruments of cautery and of the other instruments of the work as they are through addition of declaration, and through preparation of it, which cause is necessary.
But the cause for which no good artist is found with his hand in our time is because the art of medicine is long, and that it requires its practitioner to be exercised beforehand in the science of anatomy, which understands the joints of the members and their forms, and the conjunction and separation of them; and has knowledge of the bones, the nerves, and the veins, and the places of their origin.
And because of this, Hippocrates says that by name it is much, but in work little, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already spoken of that in the introduction to this book; for whoever is not knowledgeable of what we have told you of anatomy, it is not vacated that he will not fall into error by which men will die, as I have seen many of those, for some are formed in this science and they throw themselves from it without science or experience.
For I have seen an old and experienced physician open a scrofulous abscess on the neck of a woman, and cut certain arteries in her neck so that much blood flowed, causing the woman to die under his hands.
And I have seen another physician who, after he had extracted a stone from a man that was large, and came to him and drew out that stone with a piece of the bladder, causing that man on the third day to die.
And I have called it to be extracted before, and I saw by the size of the stone and the disposition of the patient that he would die.
And I have seen another physician who took a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and he treated a black slave who had a fracture in his thigh; and the physician, through his ignorance, drew out the fracture above the plague with powder and bandages with great tightness, and did not leave the wound to breathe.
And then he closed it according to his wishes, and bound it up, and let it lie for several days and commanded that we not loosen the bandage; and so it went as the thigh and the foot became pustular, and he came to perdition.
And I was called to him, and prepared to loosen the bandage, and tranquility came upon him and he ceased from his pains.
But indeed corruption had been confirmed in the member, so that I could not restrain it, and the corruption did not cease to wander about the member as the patient perished.
And I have seen another physician who opened a cancerous ulcer and it was inflamed after several days, as the malice of it grew great with its lord.
The cause of which is that the cold humor is pure, and it does not come to it with fire at all, but that there be a member which suffers that from everything it becomes hostile.
And because of this, children, it is necessary for you that the action with the hand be divided into two duties: one in operation that is associated with health, and in work in which there is peril in the greatest dispositions.
And I have already touched on that in every place of this book in which occurs operation in which there is error and fear; so that it is necessary for you to keep to that and leave that aside, so that the unlearned do not find a way to speak and to reproach you.
And therefore take your arms with diligence and the protection of God, and your patients with facility and firmness.
And use the best way by the grace of God that they may attain health and laudable success, and lay aside the terrible maladies of difficult cure, and display your arms against what makes you afraid, so that idleness in your faith and in your name does not vanquish you; for more remains to your glory, and beyond all in the realm and in the world to your sanctities.
Gauliah has already said in some of his books: “Do not treat bad maladies poorly, so that bad physicians are not named.”
And I have divided this book according to three chapters.
The division of the book begins, and first of the first part. The first chapter ordered of the head, in the forms of the instruments and of the tools of cauteries, and of what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of cauterization and puncturing, and of cupping-glasses, and of plasters, and of extraction of thorns and the like; all chapters and ordered of the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, of restoration and of dislocation, and of cure of torture and of the like; a chapter ordered of the head, in the forms of the instruments.
I will say what the quality of the humors of that cautery and of the pain of it are, and in what condition cautery is prohibited.
Therefore I say that the speech of the quality of the humor of cautery and of the pain of it is long and prolix, and subtle by science, and a secret hidden; and many wise men have spoken greatly of it, and they are disagreed among themselves, and for me a little of their speeches suffices, fearing prolixity.
And therefore I say that cautery confers universally upon every complexional matter, excepting two complexions which are complexional without matter.
In the complexional and dry matter they disagreed; for some said that cautery is beneficial to it, and the others said the contrary of that, that cautery is not convenient in disease which is by quality and dryness, for the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and it is inconvenient that the disease which is complexional and dry with what is cauterized and dry be healed.
And I say the contrary of that, that by cautery there is made humor in every disease which is complexional and dry that you see in men's bodies, for when you compare man's body to the complexions of fire, you moisten man's body.
And I say by that speech that experiment that which I have discovered several times.
But indeed no one should venture upon this topic unless he is experienced and practiced in the chapter of cauterization through subsequent exercise, and has come to know the diversity of human complexions and the disposition of illnesses in their bodies, and the causes of these, and their accidents, and the span of time of these.
But in the other complexions, there is no reason for fear, and especially in the moist and friction-based illnesses, for all doctors have agreed upon these, and are not disagreeing about the use of cauterization in them.
And know, children, that the secret of healing through cauterization with fire and the goodness of it is that fire is simple, the action of which does not spread except to the member which is cauterized, and does not affect another member joined to it, except with minor harm.
But the action of burning medicine spreads to other members that are joined to it, and strongly brings about a difficult-to-heal illness in them, and strongly hurts.
But fire, by its subtlety and the goodness of its substance, does not do that except that it expels what is superfluous; and it has been known to us through experience and through long service of study of the truth of causes. And because of this I am excused from the length of speech; and if it were not that I did not have my free will in this pause, hidden from you a secret with fire, and the quality of its action in bodies and of its expulsion by which illnesses are expelled, demonstrated with philosophical speech, which would be acute for you.
And know, children, that they disagree on the time when cauterization is convenient, and pause the best time of spring.
And I say that cauterization is convenient in all times, so that the harm from the accidental time is submerged by what is drawn out by cauterization; and especially if the cauterization is for pains or necessary pains, strong and violent, which do not endure delay because of what is feared to come from them, a maliciousness that is greater than minor harm.
And therefore do not fear in your hearts, O children, that this is not believed except by the common people and foolish doctors; for with that illness which is healed by cauterization there is not a reason to pause at all times, but they pause at what follows.
And the cause is not as they think, for cauterization is only in place of medicine which alters the complexion and dries out the moistnesses that are the cause by which pains come.
But cauterization is superfluous over medicine because of the willfulness of its strength and power of its action, and the greatness of its lord.
And it is possible for his illness to return at some time of the year, according to the complexion of the patient and the location of his illness, and the power of it, and what is prepared in his body from the accumulation of superfluities within him, and what is received by him in acquisition from foods and likenesses of those causes; unless the illness for which cauterization was administered is a subtle illness in a member with few superfluities and moistnesses, as with a toothache and its likeness, it is possible that this pain will not return to him but rather what happens to him is minor.
And it is a common saying that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine, and it is a true saying, not so that they understand it in it, for they believe that there is no healing that profits from medicine or other cause except cauterization.
And the cause is contrary to that; and the intention that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine is only when we administer methods of healing in some illnesses, and those medicines do not confer, and especially at the end of the cause we administer to them cauterization, and profit from it. And therefore we have that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine, not according to the intention which the common people and foolish doctors understand it.
And the ancients said that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they did not say this except for the equality of gold and the subtlety of its substance.
And they said that the location of cauterization does not matter, when it is not absolutely true, for I have seen and proven that it only matters in some bodies and not in others; but when you do cauterization with fire with gold, it is not declared to you because it is heated according to the amount that you want by the heat of the gold, and because it prepares itself to receive friction; and if you place over it ignition, it melts into the fire some leaves through which the artisan cuts for the occupation of that.
And because of this, cauterization is done with iron more lightly and more closely to correctness of action.
And I have already ordered this chapter on cauterization according to chapters, and I have ordered those of the head among the feet, so that elsewhere you may demand from it what you want.
The first chapter.
This cauterization confers to moisture and coldness the serving accident of which there are two causes; it is a cause of soda and multitude of fluxes, called corissa, from the parts of the ears and from the nose flowing, and multitude of sleeping, and of toothache and of the jaw, and universally of all illnesses that come through frigidity, as with paralysis and apoplexy, and their likeness in those illnesses.
The form of this cauterization is this: first, you command the patient to be evacuated by laxative medicine, the head being washed out, for three or four days, according to the power of the patient and his age and habit.
And then command that he wash his head with soap, and then rest between your knees on a low bench and place his hands over his feet.
And then pause the edge of your palm, and then press upon the edge of his nose between his eyebrows. But indeed, when your middle finger is arrived at that place, mark it with your tent, and then ignite the cauterization olive of which this is the form:
And then place it upon the place marked with your tent, in such a way that it descends, and your hand be cramped little by little, and you revolve it.
And then lift your hand lightly and look at the place; and if you see that there has been discovered some amount of the head, as though the quantity of hair, then lift your hand again, and if it does not return with that iron, with another, if it is cooled off, between while it is seen by you what I have said to you.
And then take a little salt and dissolve that over the place and let it be for three days.
And then place on it wool that is saturated in vinegar and let it be upon it while the scar of the fire is destroyed. And then heal the place with tetramedicament within while it is healed.
And they said that when Roman plaster is opened up, then the greater number of blisters it has, for it is better and more healing.
And they said that some of them that the tendon be cauterized within the bone, and have the cauterization within while some part of the tissue of the bone is burned.
And then it should be cut where it adheres to the bone, and then treated.
And others said that with the cautery they reached the nerve, which presses strongly on the bone, and this is the form of the small alpecat: And they believe that from that place the vapors of the head escape.
And if the wound has been open for a long time, it should be treated and allowed to heal over time.
But I have not seen these two types of cautery at all, except in a few cases due to fear; and I think that leaving it as it is better for health than removing it, because the head is delicate due to the continuity of its veins, as we have testified in other members, and especially when the patient's head is of a frail nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion; and using it and performing that operation is healthier.
Chapter Two:
Of cauterization of the head.
When in the whole head there is a chronic and long-lasting pain which is prolonged in the patient, and he uses leeches and cupping glasses and caputal poultices and plasters and guaiac wood and similar things, and especially if he has already been cauterized with a cautery which does not help him for any of these causes, then consider that if the patient's head is of strong creation in its nature, and not frail and finds great discomfort, then cauterize it again with another cautery after the first one. Then cauterize it on the crown of his head with a cautery so that the vein is cut. Having cut the vein and discovered it from the bone to the extent that we have mentioned, cauterize it with a cautery at the back of the head in the place which is called the windpipe, — and this is the place where the cupping glass is applied. And hold your hand over it, and do not uncover the bone, because the patient experiences great discomfort from this compared to all other cauteries of the head.
And I say this cautery in its place, because the cautery with which you cauterize the horns of the head and the occipital is a more subtle cautery than that with which the middle of the head is cauterized; and this is how it is done.
Chapter Three:
Of cauterization for non-ancient emigration.
When there is a sudden pain in the front of the head or an aching pain that extends to the eye, evacuate the head with purgatives, and administer the other treatment which I have described in the causes of diseases; and if this does not help, cauterization can be performed in two ways: either with an acute burning medicine or with iron.
But a cautery with a burning medicine is that you take a piece of wool, scorch it and put the ends of it on both sides of the head. Then pierce the location of the pain in the temple with a small lance inserted so that it is under the vein and hidden.
Then tie it tightly with a descent constriction, and leave it there for the space of three hours; then remove the wool and treat the lance-wound, and leave the wound for two or three days, and then apply cold water to the spot until it heals over.
Then treat it with an ointment so that it heals.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written about in the two treatises on burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron is done like this: heat the cautery of which this is the form:
and it is called a closed claw nail, because the head of it is shaped like a claw with some curvature, and in the middle of it there is a small projection.
Then apply it to the location of the pain, and hold your hand — so that the cautery is not burnt — and move the iron little by little with speed.
And if the quantity of it that is burnt by the specifcality of the vein is equal to the thickness of it, and raise your hand so that the artery is not burnt which is underneath, so that there is no flow of blood.
Then wet a cloth in salt water and apply it to the spot, and leave it for three days; then apply cold water to it, and treat it with an ointment so that it heals.
And if you wish, cauterize with the tip of the scalpel according to cautery, and be careful not to cut the artery in this non-ancient emigration.
Chapter Four:
Of cauterization for ancient emigration.
When you are treating emigration with what we have said about the previous cure, and what we have said in the divisions of the diseases, and the cure does not help, and you see that it does not suffice for the disease which is not abated by what we have said about the first cautery with medicine or the cautery with fire, then wherever you ignite the cautery cuttler so that it turns white, press it onto the location of the pain with a line of length equal to the thickness of the tip, or longer; and holding your hand so that the cautery does not burn the artery underneath it.
However, it is necessary for you to be careful about the continuity of the mandible which is damaged due to chewing, and that the joint not be burnt and the nerve underneath it, and that there be no cramp.
And if it is about guarding against the flow of blood from the artery which is abscised, because there is danger in its abscision, and especially to one who does not know what he is doing and is unskilled; and let the operation begin in that place.
And I will come with remembrance of the regime for accidental flow of blood from the artery according to its own way, in your free time.
And if you see that this disease does not suffice to this cautery and the patient's body is tolerable, cauterize it in the middle of the head as we have mentioned, and treat the wound so that it heals.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we have mentioned in the chapter on contraction of the artery with cautery having two tips, and this cauterization is better and more effective.
Chapter Five:
Of cauterization for pains in the ears.
When there is pain in the ear caused by cold and it is treated with caustic causes and with the other cure which I have mentioned in the division, and the pain is not eased, then ignite the cautery, and this is the one which is called puncta, of which this is the form:
Then pierce it with the cautery and ignite it, and let it be held in the environment of the entire ear as it is rotated, or in the circuit of either side if the pain is in either side. Move around with the cautery from the root of the ear a little at a time so that it touches the spot with effort.
And if the cautery of ten points in the entire ear, and those around it, and then treat the spots so that they heal.
Chapter Six:
Of cauterization for torture.
Torture which is cured and treated with cauterization is nothing but the spices which come through phlegm, according to what we have said in the divisions of the diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the area that is affected by dryness or by a nerve spasm.
When you are treating this type of torture with leeches and bloodletting and poultices, and your treatment is not working, then you must cauterize the patient in three places: first, at the root of the ear; second, a little below his temple; and third, at the junction of two lips.
And apply your cautery opposite to the affected area, because softening will not occur except in the part that is not healing.
And the method of cauterization is that you cauterize it with the cautery on the right side of the upper part of the ear, a little above the corner of the head; and in the temple another one, the length of which should be according to the length of the little finger, and apply your hand with the cautery until half of the quantity of the size of a barleycorn is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a tool of the surgeon, the shape of which is described below. But indeed more subtle is that one little bit as you see; and if it has some superficial hardness, cut a little.
And then treat the area with what is described in front of you until it is healed.
Chapter 7
On cauterization for ancient apoplexy.
When apoplexy is ancient and you are treating it with what we have said and your treatment is not working and the patient does not have a fever, then cauterize him with four cauteries: over all the corners of his head; and with cautery on the middle part of his head, as we have said; and with cautery in the neck. And the manner of the cautery is done according to what has been said above.
And he has already been cauterized over the mouth of the stomach; and it is more effective in the rear; and then let it be treated with what comes out.
Chapter 8
On cauterization for obliteration caused by phlegm.
Let the patient be given at the beginning large leeches and purifying pills by the servant.
And then shave the whole head of the patient and apply to his neck the poultice described in the treatise on poultices.
And apply that several times, for that is the manner of cauterization. And do that according to the order of the medicines which we have already said.
And if he heals well from that, then do nothing; but if not, then do to him three cauteries in the rear part of his head, which descend from the highest part of the head towards the lowest part of the neck. And place between each cautery the thickness of a finger, and then treat the cauteries with what comes out.
But if you see improvement and the patient is able to tolerate it, then do to him cauterization in the middle part; but if you see greater improvement, then cauterize him over both corners.
Then treat that until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped according to the shape described above.
Chapter 9
On cauterization for paralyses and on softening of all the body.
Let the patient's head be purified with leeches and with what we have said; then shave the patient's head and cauterize him with cautery in the middle part of his head, and with cautery on all the corners of his head, and with cautery on the rear part of his neck, and with three cauteries over the spine of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the softening of the body for too much of that and the patients tolerate it, and the disease is strong, then cauterize him with four cauteries on the spine of his back, and lastly with cautery until half a barleycorn has been burned, and lift up your hand. Then treat that until it is healed; and let this be the olive-shaped cautery.
Chapter 10
On cauterization for epilepsy.
Let the epileptic not be cauterized unless his epilepsy is caused by phlegm.
And then let the brain of him be purified at the beginning by the servant with leeches and with the other treatment which we have said in the division when the patient is large and is able to tolerate the excess of the medicines.
And if he is a child who does not tolerate the medicines, then administer poultices and masticables by the servant purifying, before that for several days, with beneficial foods.
And then shave the patient's head; and then cauterize him with one cautery in the middle part of his head according to the previous manner, and with another cautery on the back of his head, and cauterize over each corner of his head. And if the patient is strong and tolerant, then cauterize him with cauteries which we have said, having paralysis and softening of the body over the spine of the neck and over the spine of the back.
And let it be olive-shaped according to what has been said before.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a delicate cautery according to this manner.
Chapter 11
On cauterization for melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupt and moist humors and phlegm, then cauterize him with the cauteries which we have said in the section on paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is excess phlegm going down into melancholy, and the body of the patient is moist, then give him what purifies his brain according to what has preceded in the division.
And then shave the patient's head, and then make a decently made turban of linen and place it around his neck; then apply that on the middle part of his head.
And let him be sixty days old, and let him be held in every part.
And then apply a poultice; and it is more worthy of a young sheep's milk. And heat that on the fire with moderate warmth, which he endures when it is within him. And then apply that on the middle part of his head around the circle, and let him be within until he is relaxed.
And do that with the patient throughout the week once a day, and let him have the other regimen within him until he is healed; or if you want, cauterize him making small points according to his age: this is a special cautery which moistens the brain with equality.
And then apply that cold in a woolen bandage or in linen bandages.
Chapter 12
On cauterization for water descending into the throat.
When it is declared that water enters the throat through the holes which you have seen in the division, then begin and give the patient what purifies his brain; and guard him from all moistness.
And let him sip at a bath in lukewarm water for several days.
And then command that he rinses his head, and cauterize him with cautery in the middle part of his head; and then cauterize him with two cauteries over two temples if the beginning is water in two holes, or from one side if it begins from one hole.
Puncture with cautery all the veins and arteries which are below the barleycorn; and let the cauteries be in that length in the width of the temples.
And beware of blood flow when cauterizing; and if you see blood flowing from him, stop it with whatever medicine is available to you.
And let the wisdom that is in the extraction of arteries and the abstention from those and the cauterization of blood flow come to you.
And sometimes it is cauterized on the neck under the jaw bones with two cauteries recently produced.
Chapter XIII.
Of cauterization of old tears.
When tears of the eyes are old and there is an excess of phlegmatic fluid flowing through the veins and arteries, which are evident in a protruding lump on the head, then cauterize that with the cautery we described in the chapter on preventing water from going into the eyes, and cauterize it also at the middle part of the head, and with two cauteries at the neck under the temples, and with two cauteries at the neck under the jaw bones. And if there is a need for addition, then cauterize that all around the corner of the eye above the upper eyelid with a small cautery.
Chapter XIV.
Of cauterization of bad odor from the nose.
When you have treated him with what we have said in the division and your treatment is not effective, then prepare and give the patient enemas for three nights; then shave his head, and cauterize it with a cautery made of olive oil. Then cauterize that with a cautery made of clove on the two upper eyelids, just below where the hair starts, with two small cauteries.
And beware of the artery so you do not burn it.
And this is how the clove cautery is made:
Chapter XV.
Of cauterization to soften the eyelids of the eyes.
When the eyelid of the eye is softened due to illness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a lunar cautery; and this is how it is made:
And if you are treating the upper eyelids, cauterize them with two small cauteries all around, and let them be elongated from the temples. And the length of each cautery should be according to the size of the eyelid.
And do not press your hand with the cautery too much, but according to how much the third part of the eyelid is attached.
Chapter XVI.
Of cauterization of the eyelid when the hairs are converted into parts inside and close the eye.
When the hairs of the eye are converted into parts inside and close the eye, there are two ways to cauterize them: either with a cautery made of fire or with a burning medicine.
If you cauterize him with fire, command the patient to open his eyes so that the hairs are pulled out with them as they grow and are elongated; and if he closes his eyes, then you strengthen his eyelids with a bandage so that he does not rub his eyes while the hairs grow.
Then as they grow and become elongated, then place the patient's head in your lap, and press on the eyelid of the eye with your finger, with the sign being according to the shape of a dead leaf, and let the sign be near the hairs.
Then place a cotton pad soaked in egg white or in mullein tea on the eye.
Then ignite the cautery, of which this is how it is made:
Then cauterize over the figure you have marked, little by little, in small areas, until all of the surface of the eyelid is burned, which should be like a myrtle leaf, appearing completely healthy.
And the sign of the success of your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted and the hairs of the eye elevated above the puncture of the eye.
And then raise your hand and let him lie down for three days, and then place a bandage on him until the scab is healed. Then treat that with ointment until it is healed.
But if the hairs return for any reason and the eyelid is softened, then repeat the cauterization on that eye again just as you did at first.
But if the hairs are in the lower eyelid, then cauterize that until it is elevated to its natural place and elongated, and let the hairs not touch the eye.
But the cautery with burning medicine is that you command the patient to open his eyes so that the hairs are pulled out with them as they grow and become elongated, and then let it be made of linen in the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Then take sagewort and live coals, one ounce each. .I. Put them in a mortar and pestle, and mix those ingredients well, and make it into a poultice so that it is not too cold; then spread that on the linen which is made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and apply that to one eye or both; then let the patient lie down with his head in your lap.
Then hold the poultice over the medicine and press it gently so that the patient feels the biting of the medicine, for the biting is in him like fire.
And when he feels the biting, then let him drink the medicine and wipe it off with his own finger; then when the biting stops, let him rinse the eye with water, and then examine if the eyelid is lifted up as it was washed with fire or with a cut, for then it is well; and if it returns to that which the medicine was applied on the spot where the medicine did not penetrate and is blackened as your operation is healed and the eye is contracted.
Then place a bandage on him until the scab of the swelling is eradicated, and then treat that with ointment of hellebore or another so that it is healed.
It is necessary for you, after your operation, to guard against the eye being damaged by the medicine with great diligence, and if the eyelid after a few days is softened and for some reason the hairs enter the eye, then repeat your medicine on that spot of the eyelid which is softened, and precisely as you did at the beginning, and treat him until it is healed.
And this is the shape of a myrtle leaf.
And know that men' eyes are diverse according to their size and smallness, and therefore according to how your contraction is made.
And not being doubtful about the path of truth to him who is taught in this art.
Chapter XVII.
Of cauterization of a fissure which is in the tear duct of the eye.
When you cure a fissure with what we have said in the divisions of illnesses and your treatment does not work, then know that you cauterize it in this way: command the patient to lie down his head in your lap, and let someone hold his head up by the hair so that it is not rubbed or moved with his head.
Then place a cotton pad soaked in egg white or in mullein tea on the eye of him.
Then ignite the cautery, of which this is how it is made:
Which should be hollow, like a reed or a pen, and from one end is made the cautery.
And if you see that it has penetrated into the other extremity. And if you want to let it out in this way, then do so with a tent.
But the one who is hollowed out is better for your work.
Then press the fistula if it is open and bring out from him pus and dry up that fluid. And if it is not open then open it and extract the fluid from him.
And then place on him the cautery which should be well lit and hold your hand a little below the cautery at the opening of the nose so that your hand does not get burnt or the patient is not disturbed and then place the cautery in the wounds of the nose and corrupt that area.
And so if at the beginning of your cautery it goes into the bone then that is good and if not then repeat the cautery one or two times if necessary; leave it like that for three days, and then place a bandage on that area and wrap it with a drying ointment until it is healed.
But if twelve days have passed and it has not healed then place a corrosive acute medicine on it so that the bone is uncovered and scrape it according to what is convenient in its chapter.
And there is another way of cauterizing a fistula which some first said: enter the site of the fistula and open it and then place in that fissure subtle cement of which its shape is like this: melt an ounce of lead into that, and hold your hand with cement with good retention so that the melted lead does not come to the hole of that; and cover the hole on the patient's nose with cotton or water, because lead adheres to the site of the fistula and heals it with a wonderful cure.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said about cauterization and treatment then that is good; and if not then it is necessary to administer a nose piercing and reduce the fistula to the cavity of the nose according to what will come in its own place.

And then repeat that several times so that the virtue of the fire reaches the root of the teeth.

Chapter 50.
On cauterization for cancer.
When a cancer has begun and you want it to stop, then cauterize it with a circular cautery around its circumference as it is rolling around.

And it is much better that it be cauterized around its circumference with a circular cautery, as we have said, or with many cauteries.

And then join the two lips with a suture and strengthen those, and over them powder blood of dragon's blood and cut up into three parts, and place over the powder a piece of emplaster diafinicon or of another one of the emploasters that consolidate, and let him lie so for two days or for three, and then release him and change the emplaster and let him lie so that the wounds are closed by themselves, and then treat with ointment so that it has healed.
But the method of suturing is that you add the solution of continuity or needles as we have recommended in the suturing of the abdomen, or add the solution of continuity with gallbladder as you and made known.
Chapter XXVII.
Of the extraction of those causes that come between the two lips in parts inside.
Many times therefore many men in the parts inside their lips have some apoplectic ulcers hard resembling fat of worms, and some are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore you should turn the lips inside out and cut each of those hard places all around, then make an incision in the swollen part where the blood is constricted, then wash it with a pleasant wine and cure the place with something that causes constriction until it heals.
Chapter XXVIII.
Of incision of the flesh tight in the gums.
Frequently there is a growth of flesh tight to the gum which the ancients call albus, therefore if you suspend that with tweezers or something you have, and cut it at its root, letting the pus or blood run out.
Then place on the incised part some other astringent or caustic powder.
And if that flesh returns, then cut it again secondly and cauterize it, for by cauterizing it will not return.
Chapter XXIX.
Of reason of teeth with iron.
When in the surface of the teeth of the inside parts and of the hole and between the gums are tight rough scars, hard, and some times they are black and they are made yellow or green inside, through which comes corruption to the gum, and the teeth are made hard.
And therefore you should make the patient open his mouth and place his head in your lap, then press the teeth where those scars appear, and remove them as if they were sand so that no cause remains within, doing this the blackness and yellowing and others are cured until they are healed.
And if this is what is at the beginning of the cause, it is good; but if not, then you repeat the remedy on those every other day and on the second and third until it comes to the final state which you want.
And know that teeth have many needs of various remedies of diverse forms and of many figures according to your operation. And therefore the razor with which the tooth is cut from the inside part is different from the razor with which it is cut between the teeth according to other forms; and you ought to prepare these razors as you will see immediately:
Chapter XXX.
Of uprooting of teeth.
That you cure the tooth of its pain with every art and delay in total uprooting of it, for there is no cause that surpasses the growth of it when it is uprooted, for it is a sublime substance which is an obstacle to its uprooting.
But if the patient is forced to have that tooth uprooted, you must be certain which is the painful tooth, for many times the pain deceives the patient and seems to be in a healthy tooth, and you uproot it and yet the pain does not cease until the diseased tooth is uprooted, for we have seen this by operation of the gums.
Therefore when you are certain which is the painful tooth, and know well, then cut around the circuit of the tooth with a spatulate instrument in which there is some virtue, until it is dissolved from the gum all around, then pull it with your fingers or with subtle forceps little by little until you draw it out.
And therefore grab it strongly with strong tongs or hold it well between your fingers so that you do not harm it, and draw the tooth according to rectitude in manner that you do not break it, nor give great pain to the patient.
And therefore if it is not loose, then take one of those instruments and place it underneath it all around with facility and exert force on removing it in the same way as before.
But if the tooth is split or rotten, then take a piece of cloth and fill that split and tighten it with a wire at its extreme with a subtle ray so that it is not broken in the time of your constriction on it with tongs.
And take care to cut around the circuit of all parts of the gum and guard your strength so that you do not break it and tear a part from it, then return on the patient for the malice that he may be major that the first pain of him.
And take care that you do not do as some ignorant barbers do in their audacity on the uprooting of them, not administering what we have recommended; for often they bring great diseases upon men which are difficult to be tied, and their roots are all wrinkled, or some of those, or that you draw it out with some bones from the jaw as we have sometimes witnessed.
And therefore let the uprooting of it be washed in the mouth with warm wine or vinegar with salt.
And if then blood flows from there, as it often does, take some quantity of alder and sprinkle on the wound, and if that alder does not help, cauterize.
The form of the tongs and forceps subtle with which the tooth is drawn at the beginning should be of long length, with short and thick handle, so that they are not bent when you operate with them.
And they should be of such thickness of handle that when you strain on them they are not bent, they are short lengths.
And they are made of iron of India descendingly made of straight lengths, and in the ends of those there are teeth of which some enter into others and strain by strong constriction, and sometimes the ends of those are the form of a duck's beak which is hooked, it is bent, and are of strong constriction.

Of uprooting of teeth and extraction of the bones from the jaw.

Chapter XXXII.
Of the treatment of teeth that stick out above the others.
When teeth stick out and do not follow their natural course, especially in women and particularly in old people, you should consider this: if the tooth does not touch another tooth and is not possible to file or draw it back, then you must cut that tooth; but if it is attached to another tooth, then you must insert this instrument into it, whose form is as follows; it is similar to a pig's snout, and it should be made of Indian iron with an acute end.
And you must make the incision through which you will insert it into that tooth for several days according to the hardness of the tooth, and so that none of the other teeth are damaged.
And if the prominent tooth is exposed to abrasion, then file it with an Indian iron file whose form is as follows:
And let it all be made of Indian iron and its edges sharp; and let there be small pointed tips on it like files with which needles are made, with which the tooth is filed little by little over several days with ease, so that no other teeth are damaged or hurt. Then smooth it at the end, and scrape it with a razor.
And if it is a tooth whose one side is broken and hurts the tongue, then you must file that until its harshness is smoothed and flattened, so that the tongue is not hurt nor speech impeded.
Chapter XXXIII.
Of restoring damaged teeth with gold or silver foil.
When a concussion and removal occur to teeth due to impact or falling, and the patient is unable to spit it out, and you have treated it with astringent medicines but your treatment has not worked, then so that they do not fall out, they must be strengthened with gold foil rather than silver, for silver turns green and hurts after a few days, but gold never changes.
And the foil should be so thin and thick according to the amount that can fit between the teeth; and the way to make the filling is to take the foil and place twice as much between two healthy teeth, then let it grow with two twists of the foil between two damaged teeth, or if one or more, until the tissue reaches the healthy tooth from the other side. Then repeat the filling on the side where you started, and strengthen your work with ease and wisdom so that it is not damaged.
And let your binding be such that it holds the foil so that it does not escape through the roots of the teeth.
And then cut off the excess foil with tweezers or tongs, and place those in every part with gestures, and fold them and conceal them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth so that they do not hurt the tongue. And then let it be so tight like Roman armor.
But if it is loose or torn, strengthen that or those with another foil in the same way at all times.
And this is the form of teeth and the figure of the filling of two healthy and two damaged teeth, thus with their edges tied together:
And when one or two teeth are pushed back into their sockets and grow again as we have described, and become damaged again.
And it does not do this except by a skilled and wise artisan.
And sometimes it is apt to use some cause of cow bones, and it is made in the form of a tooth, and placed in the place where the tooth fails, and strengthened as we have said, and remains and serves with that for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
Of the incision of the membrane which appears on the tongue and prohibits speech.
This membrane which appears below the tongue sometimes happens naturally or accidentally due to a wound that has already healed.
And the operation on it is to open the patient's mouth, and let his tongue hang down; then lift up his tongue and insert that nerve membrane according to its breadth so that the tongue is loosened from its binding.
But if there is any hardness or knotting in it, and this occurs due to the hardening of a wound, then you must pour on an ointment and cut that through its breadth so that you have cut the membrane and the knot is dissolved.
And be careful that there is not a crack in the flesh of the lip and you cut through to the artery and blood flows out.
Then wash the wound with vinegar or warm water, or water mixed with vinegar; then let the tongue hang down, so that it is not hardened again in any other way.
But if blood flows out, then apply powdered vitriol to the spot; but if the blood stops, then cauterize the spot with a hot cautery that suits this purpose. Then let this be cured by the other causes so that it is healed.
Chapter XXXV.
Of the extraction of a ranula below the tongue.
Sometimes an aposteme occurs below the tongue, resembling a small onion that prevents the tongue from its natural movement; and when it is swollen, it fills the mouth.
And the operation on it is to open the patient's mouth wide in front of you and consider the aposteme.
And if you see it with a dark or black color, hard, to which the patient does not react, then do not touch it for it is rotten.
And if it is white and moist, then put on an ointment and insert the needle into it, and cut through all its parts; and if blood flows out when you operate, then let a poultice be applied to it afterwards with powdered vitriol so that the blood is stopped.
Then return to your work so that you may have treated it with praise beforehand, and let it be washed with warm vinegar and salt; and cure it with the other cure for this purpose until it is healed.
Chapter XXXVI.
Of the extraction of an aposteme of two ears and of that which occurs in the gum, and of other apostemes.
When there is a growth in the throat that resembles a lump, it is made up of glands that are clustered together and protrude outward, and are called tonsils. If you treat them with what we have said in the previous section and they do not heal, then consider whether the abscess is hard, dark-colored, and has little feeling; if so, do not touch it with iron.
And if it is red in color and the root is thick, then do not touch it with iron due to the flow of blood; instead, let that part mature, and either you should cut it out or it will burst on its own.
And if it is white in color, soft, and the root is slender, then that is what should be cut out.
And the operation on him is to consider, before your operation, whether the abscess has already cauterized completely or has diminished in any way; and then have the patient lie down facing the sun, with his head towards you, and open his mouth, and the servant press down on his tongue with a noble instrument of which this is the form:
And it should be made of silver or bronze, and be slender like a needle.
So when you press with this instrument onto the tongue and the abscess is revealed to you, then grab it with your forceps and pull it outward as far as you can. Another method is that you pull on one of the membranes, then pierce it with an instrument of which this is the form:
Another method is that you pull on one of the membranes, then pierce it with an instrument of which this is the form: It is similar to forceps, but the ends of it are bent so that the holes at each end face inwards towards each other, very pointed and sharp; this should be made of iron and well-tempered.
But if this instrument is not available to you, then pierce it with a spatula of which this is the form of it: And it should be sharp on one side but blunt on the other side.
And after you have pierced one gland, pierce the other according to this method of piercing, and then close up the patient's throat with a cloth soaked in water.
But if there is a flow of blood, treat it with water that has been cooked with the rind of pomegranate or myrtle leaves and similar things that restrict the flow of blood; then cure it until it heals.
And sometimes there are other abscesses in the throat besides those two tonsils; pierce these according to what I have said about the piercing of two glands equally.
I once treated a woman for an abscess that had formed in her throat; this abscess was dark in color, had little feeling, and had closed up her throat; the woman was breathing with difficulty, prohibited from eating or drinking water, and had come close to death if she had lived one more day or two.
And the abscess had grown so much that it had pushed out two bumps for a distance of two fingers.
So I cut it open with a pair of forceps and pulled it out; and it came out from him in great quantity; then I pierced the other side where I understood the other tract was, and I bound up his mouth, compressed his tongue, and placed the instrument in that abscess.
And then I cut away a great part of it; only a little blood flowed from him. So her throat was completely closed up; she began to drink water at once and then ate food; therefore I did not cease piercing that abscess many times for a long time, and there arose an abscess in the place of that one, which I pierced when it had become prolonged.
So I bled and cauterized her throat, therefore she began to vomit; and then I walked around in bewilderment and did not know what God had done to me.
Chapter .xxxvii.
On piercing of the uvula when it is abscessed.
When catarrh descends into the uvula and it is abscessed and long, then it is called perpendicular; and if in the lower part it is thick and round, then it is called a boil.
But if it is cured with what we have said in the division and does not respond to your treatment, and you see that the abscess cauterized is already thin and is round; and if it is along the length of it and is bloody, dark-colored or black, and has no feeling, then beware of piercing it, for there is danger in that.
And when you see it according to the way I have said of its whiteness and length, make the patient lie down and press his tongue with an instrument of which we have described the form; then place the forceps in the new one, pull it downward and pierce it with either of the two instruments of which we spoke about piercing two glands.
And beware of piercing it except where it is attached to the natural part by a little addition; for if you pierce it more, you will break its speech.
And after the piercing, use what we have recommended in the piercing of two glands, and cure it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the piercing of his throat, beware of using anything on him that causes fear; and the manner of cauterizing him is not with hot water, but you should lay the patient's head in your lap, then press his tongue with the instrument we have said, and then take the water we spoke about in the chapter on cauterization, and apply it with a live coal, and pour that not thick or thin, and with that fill the concavity of the instrument of which we said this is the form:
Let the end of it be concaved in which the medicine will rest just like the cup of a spoon; then place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula, and let the patient lie on his side so that drool or spit from the mouth of him due to the medicine, so that nothing goes down into his throat and thaws it.
And then hold your hand over the medicine, and express it upon the uvula every half hour until you see it become blackened and subdued by the biting of the medicine.
And if you want, take a cotton swab and wrap it around the end of the rod; then dip the rod in the medicine and insert the rod with the cotton into a tube so that the cotton adheres to the tube, and do this as many times until you achieve what you want from cauterizing the uvula; then let it be until it dries up and falls off after three or four days.
And if you see that the medicine needs to be repeated, repeat it; then after cauterizing cleanse around the uvula with a wet rod that has been dipped in vinegar, and clean what is in the circle of it from the medicine with something that is in the circle of it, and let it be washed with water rubbed, and let it be treated outside with an ointment, and let the gums be treated inside until it is healed.
And this kind of treatment is more safe than piercing and bleeding and less dangerous.
Sometimes the uvula is cured by something that is thinner than cauterizing and piercing, for it is cured by sub-smoking in this way: take calamus, hyssop, orris root, rue, Arabic thistles, camomile, bruised and similar to those of the herbs, and mix all these ingredients into one wave, then dip them in wine; let the wave be made with decent wine, and let there be in the middle of the wave a tube, over which is placed an instrument made of iron according to this way; let it be made of silver or of iron.
And the extremity is inserted into the patient's mouth until the steam reaches the uvula above the glottis, until the uvula becomes very foggy.
And then repeat the process several times until it is dry, and beware that you do not perform this treatment at the beginning of the swelling of an ulcer, for it would be too tight on the ulcer, and you should not do this, but rather reveal the ulcer to him cauterize it.
But if you do not have this instrument available, then take a cane and attach a woolen ball to its end, so that the patient's mouth is comfortable, for the heat of the fire prohibits the steam from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
On the extraction of thorns and of that which adheres in the throat, and on other causes.
Many times foreign objects become attached to the glottis or a thorn from the foot or some other cause.
So you must extract what is visible from among them by pushing the tongue with an instrument in front of it, so that you can see what is in the throat.
And what is hidden in the throat and cannot be seen, you must cause the patient to vomit before the food has digested in the stomach; for often the cause which afflicts the glottis is dislodged by vomiting; or let the patient swallow a piece of napkin or lettuce leaf or a bit of dry bread; or take a piece of dried sponge and rub it with vinegar, and then let the patient swallow it, for when it reaches the place where the thorns are located, they will be dislodged. Or take an eel and rub it with vinegar, and have the patient swallow it while it is still moving, or take a pork and when you put it in, move it around to dislodge it.
But if they are not dislodged by the causes which we have mentioned, then use an instrument of lead in this form:
And it should be larger than a little finger, and at its end there should be a concavity which you insert into the patient's throat with ease.
And hold the patient'’s head up, and beware of touching his throat bone, lest it hurt him or dislodge the thorn with your hand, or that the doctor with his hand.
But if the patient inserts it himself, because of his knowledge of the location of the cause, and pinches downward, then pull your hand with the instrument upward, as much as he is prepared to bear until it is dislodged.
Chapter 39.
On the extraction of blood which is attached to the glottis.
When the blood is medicated as we have said in the division of the cure with medicines and do not confer, then consider in the glottis of the patient at the top, for you must press his tongue with an instrument which we have recommended to you.
So if you see the blood, extract it with a small needle or a thin and sharp one gently.
And if you are not able to extract it with that, then take a hollow cane and insert it into the glottis of the patient, and place in the concavity of the cane an iron heated, and do this as many times (until the blood is dead and comes out of the glottis) and abstain from giving water to the patient all day.
And then take a cloth full of water and wring it out in his mouth, and wash the mouth with it; and let no other cause and maw enter there an hour at another time with your hand, for as soon as the blood feels the water, it will come out.
But if it is not dislodged with what we have recommended, then suffocate the patient with cumin or asafoetida by means of the instrument which we have recommended in the suffocation of the uvula.
Do this, as I will tell you.
And there is another way of operating in the suffocation of it, which is that you take an ash bowl in which are burning coals and the bowl is covered with a cloth, and in the middle of the cloth is a hole, and place the end of the instrument in that hole; then blow upon the fire from below to produce smoke; and let the patient place his mouth on the end of the cane, and close his mouth so that the suffocation does not escape until you know that the suffocation has reached the blood, for the blood will then fall.
And if it does not fall, then repeat the suffocation many times, and let the patient abstain from drinking, and eat salty causes and alums, and do not drink water, and thus necessarily the blood must be dislodged by this regimen.
And this is the form of the instrument with which blood is extracted from the glottis when it is seen; and it is like tweezers except that there is no curve in it: which is placed in the glottis, and its end is like a beaver's beak, which is narrow, in which is squeezed with lime, so that when some cause is pressed, it does not slip.
Chapter 40.
In which is the sum of the word in regard to ulcers and their sections.
Ulcers are many kinds of diseases, of various types, according to their memory, each one in this book.
And they differ in their penetration and sections by two ways, of which the one is by the kind of ulcers in themselves; and by that which comes from humidity.
And the second way is according to the places in which they occur in the body, for an ulcer that accidentally occurs on the head is different from that which occurs on the buttocks; and an ulcer in a fleshy place is different from that which occurs at a joint; and each one of them is cured by operation.
And there are also some ulcers which are not punctured but rather a maturation of the pus in them or completion.
And other ulcers which are incised when they are cruciate and not matured according to their completion, or which are near the joints, for an ulcer when it is near a joint, its matter is prolonged within that circuit, and the ligaments or nerves of that joint are greatly corrupted by it, so there is contraction of that member; or it is near a principal member, and if you delay its penetration until it is matured, the member becomes sore; or it is near the buttocks, then penetrate it that it may not fester; or when it is made in the terminal part, it should not be punctured when there is no completion, but it must be near the buttocks so that its matter does not spread to the parts of the buttocks, and a fistula is made, or that it is made in the terminal part which is not healed. But when you know the time of the penetration of the ulcer and also when it is matured.
And know that it is matured when the pain subsides and the fever ceases, and also when there is a decrease in heat and pulse, and the sharpness of the head of the ulcer, and other signs.
And because you should know when there is penetration in that pus at the bottom of the ulcer and pus comes from the cure of the eminence.
And penetration must be made along the length of the flesh or the body, if the ulcers are in the parts of the hands or feet and on the joints, on the cords of the nerves, on the tendons; and, finally, in all places where there is no reflection; for in those where it is reflected, use penetration according to that member.
But when the pustule is in a fleshy part, then it is better to make the incision in such a way that digestion is confirmed according to the proper course; because if you pierce it in front of this, the body of the pus of it and of the matter is prolonged, and much of the lips of it are hardened and the depth of it.
But some pustules are pierced according to the width of the flesh or body, or according to that from which the member needs.
And when you administer a small incision, in large pustules make a large and wide incision, or many dry incisions according to the variety of the size of the pustules.
And others are of the pustules in which the cord is hollow and cut, when it is made so that the bread and the wound is in a term that is already, as well as that which happens in trops of dubelec and of buboes.
And of those that are cut with a three-sided section; and of those in which it is cut as the shape of a leaf of myrtle, as well as the pustules of the armpits.
And of them are those in which a round section and a moon-like section are administered, and similar to them.
But that which is not head as their pustules are superficial, you pierce that with a superficial incision, or flat only.
And when the pustule is large and has collected much healthy matter, you pierce that and do not hesitate to extract all the pus at that time; having extracted a part of it, then close the pustule again on the next day, and then extract a larger part, and do this repeatedly according to the degree that it is discharged.
And especially if the patient is of cold temperament, or a woman in labor, or a small child, or an old person in decline, because the animal spirit is often resolved by the discharge of matter, and quickly dies, and you operate on them.
Therefore keep this chapter with great caution, and when you pierce those pustules, cleanse the wounds and consider that if the wound of the pustule is small, or it is a flat section, then you administer the one or the other in the usual way; and if the pustule is large, and the fissures of the incision are many, then place wool in each crack so that they come together.
And if in the pustule a part of the cord is cut or you have hollowed it out around the circuit, then fill it with old wool or thread made from linen cloth, as when you treat wounds or roots, without moisture; and close that on the third day; and then process that and cure it with what you would usually do for wounds so that it heals.
But if bleeding occurs in the hour of your operation, then you administer water rubbed with vinegar and wine mixed in which you soak a linen cloth, and place that over the site of the bleeding several times.
But if the bleeding persists, then you administer medicine and treatment, and powders which I have recommended in other places of this book and of division.
But if it is in the winter season, and the site of the pustule is a place of many nerves, then you soak powders in wine and hot oil, and place those on the sites.
And if it is in the summer, and the sites are fleshy, then you place powders soaked in water and oil or wine and oil, and do this every third day, as we have said; and then open the pustule and cleanse it and administer in its treatment whatever is appropriate for it so that it heals.
And this is what is necessary to know about the cure of pustules universally, but according to the way of distinction I have already said of each pustule in which manner is the way of its cure by particular chapters.
Chapter 41.
Of the section of pustules which come in the cord of the head.
In the cord of the head there are small pustules, and they are of the species of nodes which contain membranes that are made to them; and the species of them are many.
And of them are pimples, and of those there are which contain moisture similar to lime. And of those there are which contain moisture similar to dust, and resembling them; and of those there are which are scaly, hard.
And in the section of all of them, and in the extraction of those there is no fear, as when you extract those it does not contradict the artery.
And the operation in the section of those is that you express those with an instrument the form of which see below, knowing that it contains.
Because if what is contained is moisture, then you split that with an incision according to length by superficial incision according to this form:
And you begin the incision from line B to line G. So when the moisture is discharged, then you scrape that by which it contains that moisture, and you do not let any cause of all remain there, because repeated incisions return if anything remains there.
And then you soak the wound in Egyptian ointment, if available, and if not, in water and salt; and fill that with it and leave it overnight, because by that you erode all of what remains of that film.
And then you repeat on that wound with Egyptian ointment again and again if necessary, until you are confident that there remains no cause of that film; then cure the wound with ointment so that it heals.
And if pustule contains a fatty node, then you split that according to this form: with an incision having the shape of a cross, and cut between two lips of the incision and scrape that completely apart from every part, and use your strength in extracting the membrane which contains that.
But if the artery opposes you, then do what I have told you primarily.
And similarly do in the pustule that is scaly and in its cure as we have said; and an incision on scaly pustule is pus, because it is of little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes some of these pustules are in the head in some people who do not have moisture; and the cause is that they strike upon a pustule on the head of an old woman and stop up the pustule as if with a stone, harsh, white, which could not be pierced, and if anyone hit it, it bled.
But in other accidental pustules on the head is not this chapter, but like the pustules that come to children's heads and cause the roots of their ears, but the section of these are superficial incisions.
And make the incision of those from the lowest part of them so that the body of the matter is driven out to the lowest parts.
And then cure those with whatever is appropriate for its cure.
Chapter 42.
Of the section of scrofulas which occur in the neck.
Many times these pustules occur in the neck and armpits and armpits and other parts of the body; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.
And those which occur in the neck, and some are engendered between them, and every scrofula of these is within its own membrane, and so as in the nodes and pustules of the head, as we have recommended.
And the types of these scrofula are varied, some are stony in nature, others contain moisture, and others are malignant and resistant to treatment.
Therefore, when you see one in good condition for cutting, whose appearance is similar to the color of copper, and is smooth all around, and not attached to the nerve of the neck, or to the veins or arteries, and is not deep, you should incise it superficially from above downward along the line from G to B. Then excise it completely all around, and stretch it open with forceps, or with hooked instruments if necessary, as we have said before about abscesses on the head. Extract it little by little.
And it is important to be cautious not to cut into a vein or nerve.
And use a spatulum that is not too sharp, so as not to hurt your hand when you make the incision, or to disturb the patient; and cut only what is necessary to be cut, for if you cut into a vein or artery and are forced to stop the operation, then apply the plaster of quicklime, or other powders which stop the flow of blood, and close up the wound. Then let that which causes the pain of the abscess remain inside, and when the severity of the abscess is lessened and it starts to suppurate, When the blood is clotted.
Therefore, return to your operation until you have finished it.
And then ask with your finger if there is any cause for the other small scrofula; therefore cut into them and clean them out.
And if there is a large vein at the root of the scrofula, then you must not cut into that scrofula from its root. But before, fold a piece of cloth firmly and place it over the scrofula so that it falls down by itself without doing any harm.
Then fill the wound with old cotton which you have already soaked in Egyptian ointment, and treat it.
And if you excise the scrofula completely, then you must bring together the two lips of the wound and stitch them immediately so that no superfluity remains.
But if you see that there is superfluity because of the size of the scrofula, then you must understand how to cut into its root, and excise it and administer a stitch and that which is said.
And for those scrofula that contain moisture, pierce it with a superficial incision where you see the location of its maturation, and open an incision so that the pus drains out from deep in the body, as we have said.
And then administer an incision, apply calcium with ointment and similar to those, so that it corrodes what is caused by the corruption. And when you see the wound already cleansed, then treat it with generative ointment until it is healed.

Of the section of the pilon of the wound of an accident in the jaw.

Chapter 49.
About incisions of abscesses that occur through an artery or vein.
When an artery is ruptured and the cord which is on it is consolidated, then many times there occurs an abscess: and through what ways inflammation and abscess occur through an artery or vein: for when an abscess occurs through an artery, it extends far into the depths of the body; and when you poke the abscess with your fingers, you feel a stridor in it.
And what occurs through a vein is round to the surface of the body.
And an incision on this abscess is dangerous, and especially in those that are under the armpits, in the groin, in the neck, and in many parts of the body; and it is very serious, therefore its cure with a cautery should be avoided, and what occurs at the extremities or on the head should be avoided.
But over what occurs through them due to inflammation of the hole of the artery, split it in the cord with a long incision; and open the incision with forceps and release the artery and remove it from the tunics until it is uncovered.
And put an awl over it and make it penetrate into the other side, and tighten the artery with a double thread in two places, — which causes you to pull it out by its ends through two arteries which are in the temples.
And punch the place which is between two ligaments with a spatula so that all the blood in it flows out and the abscess is dissolved.
And administer cure to where pus is generated within so that the ligaments fall, and cure it with appropriate ointments until it is healed.
And if it is an abscess due to an incision of a vein, then you must hold with your hand what is possible for the abscess on the cord, and insert the awl under the place which you held with your hand, and make it penetrate; and tie the apostema with a double thread so that you pull it out from the other side, and ligate the apostema with a strong thread according to what we have said about the ligament of two parts with two extremities of the thread.
But if you are afraid to pull out the thread, then insert the needle under all the apostema according to the incision of the needle first, and tighten your threads in four places.
Then dry the abscess in its middle part where it has risen so that when it is dried you cut off the superfluity of the cord and remove what is bound to it.
And put powder over it which you have dipped in wine and oil, and administer the cure that is made with poultices and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 50 about the abscess which occurs due to torsion of nerves.
Just as abscesses occur in arteries and veins, and similarly abscesses occur in nerves when they occur due to excessive work or occur due to similar reasons.
And it occurs mostly in the places of the limbs or the neck, or any place where the joints are bent.
And it is a serious abscess, and it resembles the color of the rest of the body, and in its major parts are dispositions of pain and when it is pressed with force, the patient feels a kind of stupor.
And it is not an abscess which extends into the depths of the body, and therefore it is under the cord and it is bent in every direction and does not go forwards or backwards.
Therefore those which are at the joints should not be cauterized with a hot iron, because they cause great contraction when heated.
But what occurs in the head or forehead, split the cord with a spatula. But if it is small, then cut off that pus with forceps and open it at its roots.
If it is large, then suspend it with a hook, and scrape it, and stop the bleeding; and stitch the wound, and let it heal from within.

Chapter 51: Of a section of an ulcer which happens in the jaw that happens in the belly.
Some men are found to have bumps on their bellies and other parts of their bodies, which are called warts due to their similarity to mushrooms. The root of these warts is subtle, but the top is thick; sometimes their lips are affected, and they can be small or grow very large.
I have seen one such person, and in his belly were two growths resembling mushrooms, and there was no difference between them - they were both white and had a fine root.
And the lips of these warts were inverted and split, and moisture flowed from them all the time.
Therefore, I cut those, and found inside one of them, in a foot, 15 inches deep. And the other, six inches.
And the operation on their incision is that you should consider if the patient is humid and the color of the growth is white and humid with a subtle root; then you should cut it open with a wide spatula, and let it burn in the fire until it cauterizes - for often the section of it expels a lot of blood. And if it bleeds, cauterize it.
And if you see the patient is fearful and anxious, be careful not to cut with iron; instead, take a new lead seal, and tightly press it against the growth, in the manner we have described. Then leave it for two days.
Then, tighten the lead wire firmly around it; thus, you should keep tightening the lead wire until it is very firm, until it falls off by itself.
But if the growth has a thick root, then in its incision there is a cause for fear and terror, especially when it is in the belly.
But it is possible that you cut only half of it, and then cauterize it so that it does not return.
Then, treat it so that the wound heals.
And be careful not to cut a growth that is of dusky color, with little feeling, and which has a soft texture, for that is an abscess.
And I remembered the case of a man who came to me.
Chapter 52:
Chapter 52: On the cure of a hernia at the navel.
A hernia at the navel is caused by many things: or by fissures in the diaphragm which is above the belly, and its membranes or intestines depending on where they are; or from blood flowing out of a vein or artery as mentioned earlier; or from a constricted bowel in it.
But if it is because of a fissure in the diaphragm and an elevation of its membrane, then the color of the hernia is similar to the color of the body; and there is little pain, and it appears in different places. And if it is due to the hernia of an intestine, then its situation is as we have described, with more intense diversity, and when you press it with your fingers, it hides, and then returns; and it becomes strong in itself, and grows a lot because of the entrance of food and violent work.
But if it is because of moisture, then it is soft and does not hide when you press it with your hand, nor is it bound together, nor is it dry.
And if it is because of blood, then with these signs the hernia declines to blackness.
But if it is because of dry flesh, then it is a heavy hernia, large and hard, and it is formed according to a quantity.
But if it is because of a bowel, then its touch is soft.
And the operation on it is that you should consider if the hernia at the navel is because of blood from an artery or a vein, or due to a bowel; then know that you must be prohibited from curing it, for in that there is fear and terror as we taught you in the chapter where we spoke about hernias from an artery and a vein.
And if the hernia at the navel is because of an intestine or its membrane, then command the patient to hold his breath and stand straight; then press with your finger around the whole navel, and command him to stand opposite you with his chest against your chest, and surround it with a wide spatula around the place which you pressed with your finger; then extend the hernia to the sides above it with a large hook, and tie it in the circuit with strong thread or with lead wire in the decent way.
Then cut open the middle of the abscess that extends over the ligament and insert the said needle into that area, and if you have not tied it or guarded it by the ligament, then you extract the intestines and push them back into the parts within the abdomen.
And if you have tied the cloth, then take it by its end; and cut off its excess.
But if it is opposite your artery or vein, then surround that spot and return to your operation; and take two needles and place in those two strong threads and insert two needles into the circuit which you have made around the abscess in the form of a cross, making them penetrate; and then tighten the abscess into four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the spot open so that the stretched flesh can fall back down by itself; or cut off what is thinned out and let it heal, and then cure that with something that covers the wounds so that it heals.
And if the protuberance of the navel is a little flesh in there or because of moisture, then you should hollow out the abscess as we have said and draw out the blood or moisture which you find in there, and then cure that with something that covers it and consolidates the area.

Of the curing of a hernia.

Chapter 55:
Of the cure for children who are born with places of urine not perforated.

And then let it be healed with white vitriol until it is consolidated.

The shape of the forceps for cleansing, this is their shape:
