Chapter 60.
On the extraction of stones.
We have already spoken in the division of stones about their kinds and the cure of them by medicines, as well as the difference between stones formed in the kidneys and those formed in the bladder, and their signs.
And we have spoken about that kind of stone which requires operation by hand, and incision.
And the stone formed in the bladder itself is that which is lodged in the bladder.
And I will briefly narrate that with a clear exposition.
I say that stones formed in the bladder most often occur in children. And their signs are that the urine that leaves the bladder is like water in its thinness, and it appears in that sand; and the patient feels burning when he urinates, and often has difficulty urinating; and with this there may be blood in his urine.
And it is difficult to cure this in the old, but easier in children who have reached the age of 12. But it is milder for them because they are younger.
And if the stone is larger, its cure is more difficult; and if smaller, the contrary.
Therefore, when we come to the cure of this, we should first cleanse the patient with a clyster that opens up the entire passage which is in his bowels; for sometimes that opening prevents the expulsion of the stone after examination.
And then let the patient be laid on his back and made to groan and strain in order that the stone may descend into the bottom of the bladder, or jump up from its place sometimes.
And then let him make an effort with his arms and legs while he is lying on his back, so that the whole bladder may be made to decline towards its lower parts.
And then let him make an effort to urinate.
Therefore, if you feel the stone in that space, then you should cut it at once.
And if it does not come out at all by your touch, then let the patient smear oil on his left index finger or middle finger if he is a child, or the tip of the little finger if he is an adult, and insert that in his anus. And so let him expel the stone by pushing it with his finger. And gradually push that into the neck of the bladder.
And then press on it with your finger, and expel it out through the place where you made the incision.
And command the assistant to press the bladder with his hand; and command another assistant to extend the testicles upwards with his right hand, and with his other hand to press on the scrotum beneath the testicles, in the part where the incision is made.
And then insert the spatulum so that its narrow end enters first; this is how it should be done: and cut into the space between the anus and testicles, not in the rectum, in front of the left testicle; and let the incision be over the stone and the incision on the finger be in the anus, and let the incision be transverse, so that the opening is wide and the wound narrow according to the size of the stone that can be expelled from it without any compression, for fear of the finger being crushed in the wound.
And know that there is some stone whose angles and edges make its emission difficult for that reason.
And that is like a grape in shape, and it is round, and it is easier to expel from it.
Therefore, if it is the kind whose angles and edges are sharp, insert it into the incision.
And if it is not easily expelled, then let you push on that or tighten it with a decent grip, the end of which should be like a file by which to tighten around the stone, and not slip off it; or insert below it a slender instrument with a curved end.
But if you cannot move it, then let you enlarge the wound a little, and if you see blood flowing, stop it with burnt vinegar.
But if there are more than one stone, then at the beginning push your finger to the mouth of the bladder, and then insert it over them; and then push in a little bit there; and do likewise if there are more than two.
And if the stone is very large, then it is folly to insert that with a great incision, for there are two causes why this happens: either because the patient dies or he develops continuous urination because the wound is not completely healed.
But let the assistant expel it when it is expelled, or insert it into the fracture with forceps so that he can extract it in pieces.
Therefore, when you have finished your operation, then fill the wound with cotton wool and dragon's blood, and tighten it. And place a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or rose-oil and water, over it so that the wound may be cleaned by cauterisation.
And then let him lie on his back, and not loosen the bandage until the third day.
And when he has loosened it, wash the wound with water and oil, and then cure that with ointment of diafenicon and ointment of diabasilon until it is healed.
But if the wound becomes hot and ulcerated, and it seems as though the blood has congealed in the bladder, and there is no urination (and that is called by the egress of blood with urine), then insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood, for if it remains, it will provoke corruption of the bladder and putrefaction.
And then wash the wound with sharp wine, water and salt, and apply to it all manner of remedies, by which means he will be cured until he is healed.
And let him lie on his back during all the cure, and you apply both testicles and adjust the medicines which are laid upon the wound.
And if the stone is small and lodged in the urethra, and there is no urination, then cure that by what means you can insert it before the incision is made, for there are many remedies which have been proved to be effective against this; and indeed you can use an iron punch covered with velvet, of which this is the form:
triangular in shape, pointed, made on a stick.
And then take a punch and insert it into the urethra below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
And then insert the iron into the urethra, piercing with ease until the punch reaches the stone. And turn the punch with your hand around that small stone little by little, and pierce it from both sides so that you can penetrate it from the other side; for the urine is then absorbed.
And then press your hand over the opening so that it is tight around where the stone was before the urethra was pierced, for that is cramped, and there is no egress of urine, and the patient is healed.
But if this cure is not prepared for any reason which prohibits it, then insert an iron below the stone, and another iron above it. And then you light a fire on the stone in that groin between two ligatures. Then take out that object. Then untie the ligament and clean the clotted blood which is caused by the plague.
And the ligament of the bladder is not necessary except to prevent it from returning to the vesica, and the other ligament above except that when the bladder is untied the cord returns to its place and cures the plague.
And for this reason when you tie the bladder above, you must lift the cord to the parts above so that it returns to its proper state and cures the plague as we have said.

Chapter 62.

Chapter 65:
On the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The cause of this hernia is due to an opening that occurs in the diaphragm extending over the belly, from which some intestines protrude through that opening.
And this rupture is either of the diaphragm or due to an incision there, and these two types occur for various reasons: due to a blow, or from jumping, or from shouting, or from lifting something heavy, or from similar activities.
And the sign of this, when it is due to an extension of the diaphragm, is that it occurs gradually over a long time, and not suddenly, and there is swelling underneath the skin due to the diaphragm compressing the intestine.
And the sign of this type is that it causes intense pain right away; and there is a diverse appearance of swelling below the navel, and this is due to the protrusion of the intestine, and the body is pushed out at the edges of the diaphragm.
And sometimes the intestine slips out with its covering, and this type of hernia is called intestinal hernia with a sac.
And sometimes it occurs due to hernia with a sac, and sometimes there is a tear in the intestine, and it gets stuck there, and this causes great harm to the patient, and sometimes it causes intense pain especially when it is pressed upon.
And in treating this illness with iron, there is fear involved because it is easy to damage the intestines.
And the method of performing this operation is that you command the patient to draw in his intestines with his hand into their cavity if he obeys your instructions, and then have him lie on his side between your hands, and lift his legs up.
And then you should tighten the skin that covers the testicles at the top, and cut all around the testicles with your knife; and then insert two hooks into the incision to pull it open according to what you command them to do.
And if the incision is as large as it needs to be so that nothing of that which has slipped out is left behind, then you should close it up.
And then you should remove the skin that covers the diaphragm under which the testicles are located, and reveal the white and firm diaphragm underneath in each area.
Then you should put your finger into what holds the testicle, in what is between the white diaphragm and the diaphragm below, and between the diaphragm according to its location, and release it from the adhesion that is in the back part of the abdomen.
And then you should hold the skin of the testicles at their openings, and extend the white diaphragm with your left hand, and lift up the abdomen with the diaphragm to the side of the incision; and command the assistant to extend the abdomen inwards while you release the adhesion that is behind it with a complete solution.
And close with your fingers so that there is no cause for the intestine to be wrapped in the white and firm diaphragm. But if you find any cause for it, then you should push it back into the abdomen.
And then cut at the end below the scrotum through a section from which blood and pus will flow, as we have recommended in what we have said.
And then administer wool soaked in oil, and place it on the wound; and administer the bandage which we have recommended (and sometimes the scrotum is cauterized white, which we have said close to the cut of it they fear the flow of blood, because many times these occur) and leave the bandage so that by itself it falls.
But if the falling of it is delayed, then let water and lye be heated, and place it on that area so that it falls according to what we have said.
And then cure the wound with another cure for wounds so that it heals.

And then strengthen the white scrotum which is below so that it is uncovered.
And then take the band and place it over the prominent place of the scrotum, and press it on the abdomen.
And then sew the two prominent places on the end of the band of the scrotum, and adjust one of those to the other with the stitch.
And then cut the end of the band, and do not cut into the scrotum at all, nor extend the hole, nor cause anything else so that you have knowledge in the cure for intestinal hernia.
And then cure it with the cure for wounds, and as you insert the threads, cleanse that and restore the wound so that it is consolidated; because the scrotum is contracted and the eminence of the rupture is not adjusted.
And cautery is better in this place because it is closer to health.
Chapter 68.
Of softening of the scrotum of the testicles.
Many times the scrotum of the testicles in some men is softened because the flesh that is between there is softened, and it is weakened by the guard of it.

Chapter 69.
Of castration.
Know that castration in our law is excommunicated, but by cause it has been read to me that I remember it in this book; and it is not necessary to remember it except for two ways, of which the first is so that it is in the science of the doctor when it is questioned about it, who knows the cure of it when it happens; and the other way is that we many times have need of castration of other beasts because of our young ones for them, as of horses, and of oxen, and of pigs, and of those similar to the beasts.
And so I say that castration is done in two ways: or by tearing, or by cutting at an incision.
But in that which is done by tearing, the way of the operation is that we let the beast swim in water so that its genitals are softened and they hang. And then take those with your hand so that they are resolute and do not appear beneath the touch.
But in castration done by incision and cut, take hold of the scrotum of its testicles with your left hand.
And then ligate the suspensory ligament, and insert on each hole with one cut so that two holes are processed.
And then insert those pus which you will have scooped out. And do not let any cause happen on them of the scrotum, except for the subtle scrotum which is over the veins.
And this way of castration is better which is done by tearing because tearing leaves no cause to the testicles so that the beast desires copulation.
And then cure the wound so that it is healed.
Chapter 70.
Of the cure for hermaphrodites.
Hermaphrodites in men are according to two ways: the one is when or appears in that which has the space between the scrotum of the testicles, in that one which is between two testicles figure a clitoris of woman in which there are hairs.
And sometimes from that urine in the scrotum of the testicles.
But in women it is one species: and it is that the clitoris of the woman over the foreskin is thus like two testicles of man a little bit of all are joined at the entrance of the vagina thus with the foreskin of man and two testicles.
And the cure is of three species: of two of men and one species of women. And it is that take hold of those flesh tightened so that the pressure of that escapes, and then cure those with the cure for the other wounds.
And in the second species of men of which urine is in the scrotum of the testicles, there is no cure nor operation on it.

Chapter 72:
Of the treatment of elephantiasis.
Elephantiasis is when the vagina of a woman is not perforated, or the opening is small. And it can be natural or accidental.
Accidental is due to a previous illness, and this happens when the flesh is very tight or there is an issue with the bones or they are narrow.
And it can happen in the groin of a woman, or in her ribs, or in the upper or lower abdomen, and it is prohibited from coitus and pregnancy, and menstruation is very much prohibited.
And its knowledge is gained through observation by the midwife, if the pregnancy is obvious, then it's clear; but if it is not obvious, then you should feel for it with your fingers or with a rod.
But if the pregnancy is due to a narrow canal and it is close to both labia, then you should cut into it and rupture it.
And it is where you place powder on both labia, and then you place your fingers between the lips in order to push.
And the woman should be on her back, with her legs extended and spread wide open, then you should also spread both labia widely with force so that the narrow canal is broken and the pregnancy is opened up.
And then take wool and soak it in oil and place it on the wound, and keep it moist for a day so that the incision does not close up.
But if the canal is wide and thick, then you should insert it with a broad spatula or a blade of myrrh.
But if the pregnancy is due to a hernia, then suspend it with ointment and insert that.
And you should have the medicines that stop the flow of blood without biting, like astringent medicines, and dragon's blood, and incense, adjusted with egg white.
And then administer a broad bandage of lead so that the wound is not loosely closed.
And hold it for several days and administer cool water.
Then it should be treated with the other cure of consolidation until it is healed.
And sometimes there are accidents in the mother's childbirth of other flesh, and then you should insert it according to this method. But if it is due to scurvy, then there should be no contradiction with iron in any way.

Chapter 75:

Of cure of the anus of the child not perforated.

Regarding the treatment of fistula's that originate from there:
The fistula's that originate near the beginning, discharge pus which is thin and watery and originate near the anus or in the space between the buttocks.
And there is one or several fistula's.
And when that hardening becomes old and discharges thin white or yellowish pus, then some of these fistula's penetrate.
And these are known by what exits from them through the exit and by the flatulence: and sometimes the patient passes gas through it, and usually it is pus that exits from there.
And from these fistula's some sometimes penetrate into the bladder and into the urethra: and there are some that penetrate into the joint of the hip and into the root of the tailbone.
And how you know a fistula has penetrated into the anus and not penetrated is that you put your finger inside the anus, and you insert a thin piece of ram or iron into the fistula when it is not painful.
But if in that one there is pain, then insert into it a thin piece of lead or horse hair while you feel the piece or the hair with your finger.
And if you do not feel anything at all or there is no sign of digestion nor flatulence nor gas, then know that it has not penetrated, and then go to the treatment.
And when the fistula has penetrated into the bladder or urethra, then it is indicated by the exit of urine and a prohibition of it to that place until the wound is healed.
But if it has penetrated into the joint of the hip or into the bone and there is pain in the circle around it, then the sign of it is that you put a ram's horn into it if in the bone there is no pain, and there is pain in the circle of the joint: and from it pus exits all the time; and medicine does not help it nor is it healed with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistula' there is no cure, and the treatment of them is the labor and vanity of foolish doctors who do not understand it.
But in the one that is not penetrating and does not become old, healing with iron is expected according to what you have been told, and what has already happened and been tried.
And let the patient lie on his side with his upper body elevated and his legs bent upwards under him, with his buttocks inclined towards his stomach, then insert a piece of lead or ram's horn into the fistula until you know the location where the horn enters.
And if he feels pain in his neck, then insert your finger into his neck.
And if you feel with your finger the horn already inserted through it pushing forward and there is something between your finger and the fistula, then know certainly that it has penetrated: therefore do not work on that one, for there is no healing in it as we have said.
But however some say that it is healed in some people.
And the medicine by which there is hope of healing in him is that you apply cauterization gently according to the size of the fistula, as it is preceded, and insert that fire into the fistula until it reaches around his neck: and then repeat that two or three times until you know that all those fistula' which are like a feather of bird have been burned out.
Then heal that one with hot water wrapped in cloth until all those meats which are burned out, and then heal that one with medicines that generate meat and consolidate, and if it is healed, well: but if not, then it will not be healed in any time by another treatment.
And if the horn enters and does not penetrate into your finger which is in the anus, and between that and the horn there is a space of meat or burning, then let the fistula appear at where it breaks through the surface of the skin: then dry up the skin from where the fistula starts, and you dry them up with the horn, and let it be in the fistula until the incision reaches the place where the tip of the horn entered, and by the horn is freed and cut.
Then cleanse those fistula' which are like a reed of bird and do not drain from them any cause.
But if blood flows from it between you and your instrument when you press those meats, then the root that is and the pus of the wound is cauterized with fire or with sharp medicine, for cauterization with fire dries up two good humors: it is a drying up of those meats which are pressed, and an abscission of blood from the heat of the humors.
Then heal that one with hot water wrapped in cloth or with raw cotton until the place is healed over with hard, generating meat which consolidates.
And it has medicines in which there is constriction and drying up: when then it is restored, it is healed and you should not fear an infection.
But if the fistula has penetrated into the foreskin of the anus and is elongated from it over the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the anus and examine it.
And then if you feel the horn and between you and it there is burning or meat, and it is close to penetration, then there is not an operation in him unless by way of confidence and hope.

On the sewing of hemorrhoids in which blood flows in the incision of those.
Hemorrhoids are of two types, because either they are between the buttocks like charcoal, or they are like grass fat. And some of them are small and large, and blood flows from them at all times, and they are one, and are found, and outside the anus, and on its edges.

Healing of Wounds:
You have already said in the division of the healing of wounds what was fitting for the book, and I say thus the healing of those with hand and medicine according to need.
Therefore I say that wounds are various according to what caused the wound and according to the location on which the wound occurred.
And the causes by which many wounds are made are thus with stone, or incision of sword or knife, or impact of lance, or staff, or arrow, or similar to these from many causes.
But the wounds that are in the locations of the body are thus with wound that falls upon the head, or neck, or foot, or belly, or throat, and similar to those in the limbs.
And I will discuss the healing of some wounds so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other wounds.
And I will begin with simple wounds of the head, for the memory of the composite wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say that when a simple wound to the head occurs and there is no fracture, then consider: if it was from impact of stone or similar, and the vein has just been severed and there is a large wound and you fear an abscess will occur, then cut and tie the cephalic vein at once, and do not delay this; and draw out blood according to the amount of the injury's strength unless some cause prohibits phlebotomy, especially if the blood flowing from the wound is little.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part; and keep the patient from replete and wine and heavy foods.
But if some cause prohibits from the diminishment, then let it be lessened from the food and not approach meat or wine.
And place upon the wound, if it happens to him an abscess, a poultice steeped in warm oil or wine in which is mixed stiptic powder.
But if it is secure from abscess, then place upon the wound, if it is fresh with its own blood and the air has not changed that powder which I will soon describe.
And if the air has already changed that one, then place upon it some ointment until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or is from incision of sword and similar, and its lips are not closed by stitches, then close those with stitches according to how I will describe about their stitches.
But if the wound is uncovered of bone and pen, and there is no retention of it but a small suspension, then insert that one and properly heal the wound with medicines and restore the meat by command to the patient that he abstain from foods in which are coarseness: heads and formen skin and similar to them, until it is healed.
And if you find in the bone putrefaction and it does not adhere to the bone, then insert all that is rotten and throw it out, and properly heal that one.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood can flow and it is not severed by medicines, then you inquire about the artery, and if you find that one unsevered, then you insert into it with spatulum, or ligature that one; and if necessity demands that we not do as we have said, then cauterize until the blood is severed.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then the cause of them is leeching; therefore you powder over those this powder, for it will harden before the air changes those.
And the manner of the powder is this: take oil of unshorn ram, dragon's blood, and not-extinguished ash; be all pounded and sifted, and let the wound be filled with it, and be constricted by good compression until it adheres to that, and be coagulated by its own blood.
And then bind that one above with lint and let it be bound, and do not loose it while it is like this unless an abscess occurs, for the medicine will not heal until it is healed.
And sometimes use only cautery when you do not have unshorn ram and dragon's blood at hand.
And do this operation of binding in large wounds when it seems that it should be placed upon those and are tight, and are fresh with their own blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, and especially when it is in summer time, then there is no intent to the position of this powder, but then heal those with what we have said, therefore you place upon those some ointments digested, or that you place upon those plasters made from flour of wheat with water and honey until they are soft; and then heal them with the other healing within they are healed.
But if it happens that the wound is fracture of bone, and it is small, then you extract that with forceps.
And I will tell the healing of fractures of the head in what is piercer.
Of wounds of the neck:
But if the wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in medicine, and it is among the wounds of the head when it is simple.
But if the nerve of the neck is cut or artery, then if it is nerve, there is not in it artifice except that it be avoided healing of it with anything that makes vehement stiffness, therefore as cinchona and zeki that is vetiver oil and similar to them because they harm nerves and against that. And do not place upon the wound any cause of all heat, for the substance of nerves is heat and its continuation is in the brain, which is the source of all members.
And if that which is healed by medicines cooling, therefore as it is washed with warm water, some vinegar infused in oil or not, and thucine when it is washed with warm water and the other stones minaret according to this manner, and ointments humid.
But if the wound is large, then administer stitches, or narrow its lips, and then heal that one with lint until it is healed.
But if the wound has depth or occurs to it the low pus broken, then pierce that one in its location but not deep-punched.
But if in the wound an artery has already been cut and blood flow is achieved, then either ligature or cauterization of it should be performed, depending on what your need requires.
But if the ribs of the throat or all of them have been cut and the veins of the neck have been preserved, then you should stitch the edges of the wound over the throat with a needle and not touch it; instead, you should pull that area gently back into its natural shape and tie it tightly with a decent bandage; and you should treat that area with a poultice until it is healed.
But if the wound has caused a fracture in the bone, then you should probe it and extract any bone fragments that you can; and what you cannot remove, let it be lanced so that the wound may be cleaned out, separated from the bone, and the pus drained away.
And if the wound is fresh, then dust it with the powder that has been recommended to you. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with what will be mentioned later in its own chapter.
If it is a spear or halberd wound and you see the depth of it, then consider whether there is any wind coming from the patient; then know that the wound is fatal.
And if it is not deep and the wound is fresh with its blood, then you should put on a powder at the first hour and not compress it so that the blood is not held back in its depth but returns to the heart and the patient is relieved; rather, apply an attracting poultice and loosen it twice a day.
And if the poultice is not available to you, then put into the wound a piece of old cloth so that it draws out what is in there; and have the patient sleep on his back with his legs raised so that what is inside can flow out.
But if three days or more have passed and the patient does not experience spasms or a bad tremor of the heart or a tightness in the chest, and you see other good dispositions, then know that the wound is healed; and then treat it with hot water and the other remedies until it is completely healed.
But if its healing is delayed and the wound has been open for a long time, then know that a fistula has formed; treat it therefore according to its own chapter.
And if the wound is from a spear or halberd, and it is simple on the surface of the foot or leg, then treat it with what precedes the sewing closed, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
But if it has caused a fracture in the bone and only fragments remain inside, then you should turn the wound over to extract those fragments, if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then let them be lanced so that their extraction is eased.
And for other accidental wounds in the other limbs, the treatment is what we have already said.
Of wounds to the abdomen:
The disposition that occurs in the abdomen is either large or small or middling.
And a small disruption is made difficult in another way: and you will find that reducing them is more difficult if you do not close off the intrusion of them into their location immediately, for when they are opened up, they are swollen so that it is difficult to put them back inside.
And therefore the best disruption is middling, for in that one the reduction of the intestines is not difficult, and so it is with the two other kinds mentioned before.
And know that the sewing closed of the abdomen is according to four methods; however, two sutures are common that apply also to sutures of the other wounds of the body.
And two others are specific to abdomen wounds, but they are more proper in sewing closed the abdomen.
And I will recount to you each one with its explanation.
Therefore I say that when the wound is small and some cause of the intestines comes out through it, and its reduction is made difficult in one of two ways: either because of the smallness of the disruption as we have said, or because the intestines are given inflammation by the coldness of the air.
And therefore when that is so, then you should warm it by immersing a sponge or cloth moistened in warm water alone, or you could mix in squill, and cypress, and spikenard, and myrrh with the water and apply it to the intestines until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes it does this in the wine in which there is stypticity, and it is better for resolving inflammation than just water.
And therefore when you see the inflammation is resolved, then you should moisten the intestines with water in which there are mixed alder and mallow, for by this means the intrusion of it is eased for little cause.
But if the intestines are not drawn back by this treatment, then you should cut the disruption a little with an instrument by which the ulcers have been cut.
And the first treatment is best when it is not cut and there is no need to dry it out immediately nor any shortage of food.
Of the instrument, know its shape:
The one part of it is curved and acute, and the other part not acute, and its tip is not blunt but sharp; before, it is obtuse a little.
And therefore when the wound is enlarged and there are intestines coming out of it, then you should have its reduction according to the natural shape of it in its proper location, if that is possible, for that is best.
But when the disruption is wide and it is in the lower part of the abdomen, then you should have the patient lie on his back with his legs raised higher than his head; and if it is in the upper part, then let him lie on his back with his head and feet higher than his middle parts.
And likewise if the disruption is in one of the two parts of the abdomen, then pay attention and consider always that the part in which there is the wound be higher than the other.
And therefore you should do this with large wounds and middling wounds.
And for small wounds, do what is prepared for you; and indeed the ministering of it should be between your fingers, which hold the wound with its own hand and adjust its edges, and indeed uncover from it to that which will sew it shut, so that any cause for sewing is removed.
And this is the common way of sewing: you take a needle and thread according to the length of the wound's size; then let go of the end of the disruption with your thumb, and draw in the thread one, so that you do not put the thread through both ends of the wound, by the fingers, within which hold the wound with its own hand; and you have joined together the two ends of the codon by the two ends of the ciphac, and so you have made four stitches.
And now take a strong thread and double it around the needle, and tie some knots at both ends, and then bring the two lips of the wound together with a decent tie; and leave the thickness of the finger, and insert another needle there, and tie that also with thread in the same way as you did with the first needle; and keep doing this if it is necessary with needles until you have completed the whole length of the wound.
And let the needles be neither too large nor too small, for thin needles easily penetrate the flesh; and thick ones are difficult to insert in the wound, and therefore let them be smaller in thickness but larger in length.
And let the needle not be placed at both ends of the wound nearby, so that the flesh is not cut too much and the wound opens before it has healed.
And they must not be prolonged with a stitch so that the wound is not prohibited from healing.
And now insert the ends of the needles so that they do not bother the patient when he sleeps; and place over the stitch powdered herbs spread out on both sides, which will hold the ends of the needles in place; and leave them there until you know that the wound has healed.
And this method of stitching with needles is most convenient for small wounds, as it only requires one or two needles to be stitched around them.
But the second stitch is done as follows: you must stitch the four ends, of the wound and two ends of the head in one go, with a needle which has thread wrapped around it, both thin and thick.
And then when you have penetrated all four ends with the needle, return the needle to the point from which you started, so that the thread is pulled back towards the wound, so that the stitch is like a stitch of cloth, and the cloths are tightened.
And leave between each stitch the width of a little finger.
And this is the stitch for men.
And with this stitch I healed a wound that a man had received on his belly, and it was a stab wound of more than one palm in length, and the rupture was of half a palm on the right side of the belly, and the disruption was in the middle of the limb; and then I returned him after the intestine had been outside for 24 hours.
And the wound was healed within 15 days or so, and he recovered, and then he left after a few months, and is doing well in his health.
And other doctors had judged that he would not heal; but by miracle he was healed, for I did not cure him with ointment, as it was a place where ointment could not be applied or any medicine put on, but I placed over the wound a cotton pad twice a day and washed it often with honey water until he was healed.
But I will tell you of two kinds of stitches proper according to the account of the Word.
And you must start by stitching the wound closed from the outside in.
And therefore as the needle penetrates the wound and into that part from outside to inside, leave the end of the head in the part from which you started; and then let the other end of the head penetrate from the inside out at the other end of the limb.
And when you have done that penetration, then let the other end of the head penetrate another time in that meteyssa extremity of the limb from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the head that is on this side, and let the needle penetrate at the other end of the limb from the inside out.
And let that penetration by which you are making the head penetrate in the extremity of the limb in which it is, penetrate all the way through.
And then start from this side and stitch that with the end that is of the head on the other side, and withdraw the needle from the wound which it has penetrated, and then return it to that wound and stitch the extremity of the limb that is on the other side with that extremity of the limb; and withdraw that from the wound which it has penetrated.
And do this once and then again, and do this once more so that you sew up all that which is outside according to that example.
And the sum of this method of stitching is that you sew with a stitch of skin, so that you stitch the head one time from the end of the wound, and leave it in there until you have completed it on the other side.
But the second proper stitch which I teach is called Gualia's stitch, and it is of a limed kind that is recommended for one who is ill, so you sew it according to similarity in which all parts are joined together conveniently by nature; and the end of one is applied to the end of another, and this is done according to how you see fit.
Let the needle be placed in the end of the limb from which you start towards the outside, and let it penetrate to the inside in the sole, and leave two ends of the head; and then turn the needle around and let it penetrate from the outside to the inside at two ends of the limb.
And then turn those around, and let some of them penetrate from the inside to the outside at two ends of the limb on the opposite side.
And this method of stitching is better than common stitching, and it is the stitch by which previous memory is recalled.
This is Gualia's limed kind.
And they say also that stomach stitching is straightened with two needles, so you insert in these a thread, and start from where you are towards the other side according to sewing of those whose hearts are joined together equally.
And know that when a rupture is in the middle of the belly, then the stitching of it is more difficult than of other parts of the belly.
But the game of medicine of wound is judgment of other wounds.
The cause of which is when you know the wound to be recent with blood before air alters it, and reduce the intestine and stitch it up and dress it decently, then pause over that incarnative powder.

But if you do not have the medicines, then pause over that at once, for when it begins to discharge pus, wash it well and change that twice a day, as you teach him, until it is healed, for you must not indulge in other medicine in different conditions when wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound communicates with the principal limbs, then submerge lion wool in oil of tempered quality, or in rosin oil, and pause that over the parts which are between the angles and under the sockets.
And if you feel pain or putrefaction in your intestines, when it is many times thus, let it be cauterized with black styptic vinegar, and especially so if the rottenness is from the intestine and has made an exit penetrating into the cavity of it.
And know that the one that is in the large intestine is more difficult to heal than the one in the small intestines.
And it is difficult to heal because the intestine called "gland" does not receive healing from any plague that enters it, and this is due to the great multitude of veins that are in it, and because of its magnitude, and because of the subtlety of its body, and because of its proximity to the nature of nerves.
But if that which proceeds from the plague is the membrane, and you find it recently affected, reduce it according to your expertise, by which you reduce the intestines equally.
But if it has passed into space and is already black or green, then you should apply gall to the place where it has turned black, so that blood comes out in a flow, for there are veins and arteries in the membrane.
Then cut into that which is the other side of the membrane and attach two ends of the gall to the area below where the plague came out, so that on you is lessened the attraction of it due to the cutting of the membrane and emission of the root of the plague.
Remembrance of plagues when they occur in the intestine.
When a disruption occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible that it can be restored in some people, for I saw one who had been plagued in his belly by lancing and the plague was of the right side of the stomach, and the plague was ancient and made a fistula, and he was made to drink and belch.
Then I followed the healing of him, and it was believed that he would not be healed, but I continued to sutilize that within which healed and the place is consolidated.
And I saw the place already consolidated, so I went upon the patient that he got over the evil in his belly; and no one got sick from that evil accident and he resumed eating and drinking, and also entered into bath and was exercised in his wits.
And some experienced ones already said that when a plague occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be bound according to this manner: and it is that you take gum from great pods; and then adjust the two lips of the plague and place one of those gums which have an open mouth over the two lips of the plague; therefore when you put that on and press its mouth, cut off the pod of it, for it adheres and is not dissolved.
And then place another gum near the first one, and do not cease to do this with gums one after another according to the quantity of the whole plague.
And then reduce it and bind the plague; and let those pods remain attached in the intestine until the intestine is healed; and do not harm the patient.
And it is possible that the intestine be bound with a subtle thread which is made from the intestine of an animal annexed to it, for it is more in needle; and it is that you take the end of these intestinal threads, and it is well stripped, and then bind the end of it with a thread of it, wrapped, and then insert that thread in needles and sew up with it the disruption.
And this manner of sewing with gum is not but according to way of confidence and hope.
But if the plague is great and broad, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, for then there is not but subtilization, and not healing of it altogether.
Of healing of ulcer and fistula.
Know that all plague and apostema when it becomes old and turns into an ulcer, and is not consolidated and has a root all the time and is not cut off, then in any member it is, universally it is named fistula, and we call it azucem.
And fistula according to truth is knotted flesh, hard, white with which there is no pain and at which it is hollowed out as it is hollowed out from a tooth; and because of this it is named some nodules, that is pain.
And it is in some people moist, which has a root; and strongly the moisture is cut off in some hours. And sometimes this moisture is moved, and it is little, and it is thick, and it is subtle.
And I have already shown all those causes, and the signs of them, and their healing with medicines in the division.
And so that I make you know the healing of fistula which is made by way of operation with hand.
And know that fistula occurs in all parts of the body; for some of them reach the great veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the brain, or the intestine, or the bladder, or one of the ribs, or to any of the vertebrae of the back, or to any of the joints composed by composition as of the hand and the foot; and those are which give proximity to the principal member and their likes.
And so that one of those fistulas which are according to this manner is of diseases of difficult healing, and does not receive curing, and occupation with it is work and ignorance.
And if there is any of the fistulas whose depth does not reach those places which I said, and you hope for and trust in its healing, and you want to come into knowledge of it, then take a probe of iron or steel, and if the fistula goes according to rightness, and you examine that with it.
But if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate that with a probe of subtle lead, for lead by the lightness of its body is drawn away and curved according to the tortuousness.
And if the fistula has terrible movements, it is not possible for you to signify about it with probe.
Then christen the hole by its holes, for the moisture by which you christen that comes from the other holes and flows from them.
And then inquire carefully, according to what manner it is possible for you to know if here there are bones or nerves, and it is the hollow of the fistula distant or near, or it is a fistula to which its holes are moved and this over all by the sum of your strength, by your inquiry with hand on the place, and its likes according to the signs and causes accidental to the apostema.
And when you have stood upon all those causes with true state, then go to the healing according to confidence: and it is that you consider then if it is an apparent fistula near or in a place saline, distant from joint, or of nerve, or of artery, or of vein, or one of the places which I said to you and told.

About the treatment of hemorrhoids and the quality of the enema with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in some fleshy parts and the space of the abscess is prolonged as it collects pus, and then it is broken and punctured, and the pus is discharged from it, and it remains empty like a vessel, and the codicle that is on top of it dries up with a thin membrane, and there has not been reached to a deeper corruption, nor has it impressed on bone or nerve or ligament, it is called sinus, and is not named fistula.
But if the space is prolonged as corruption impregnates in some part, then it is called a fistula and not sinus.
And its treatment is that you cut off the entire codicle as it is rolled up, and especially when it is thin and done with a cloth, and cauterize so that it does not adhere to the place by its corruption.
But if you expect the codicle to adhere and that corruption has not reached its consequences, and in the codicle there is space, then you treat it with an enema.
And it is considered as a sinus, and if it is large, and the breath that comes from it is fetid, then enema that with an Egyptian medicine which is of oil and sour wine and thinnest honey received in parts, and are put into a vessel, and are cut over the fire until the medicine becomes hot and reaches the consistency of honey.
And then you take from it what is necessary and dissolve it with water and honey; and enema that sinus with that, and plug the opening of it, and let the medicine stay in there for the quantity of two hours.
And then pull that out with caution; and do that for so many days until the sinus is purified and the shame is healed.
And sometimes it is enema with ash water when you do not have that medicine; and take ashes from a vine, and put on it water; and then pour that and enema that sinus with it until you are convinced that the sinus has stopped.
And if the patient has no tolerance for the Egyptian medicine or ash water, then enema that with water and honey mixed with thin thinnest honey, and enema that sinus with wine and honey mixed, because of the property of honey is that it purifies and washes, and the wine makes the sinus adhere, and especially when in the wine there is surplus of astringency and dryness.
And when you do that several times and you are convinced that the sinus has healed, then enema that with what makes the flesh in it grow.
And take some thin dye and dissolve that with roasted oil and astringent wine, or enema that with some medicines and other dyes that I have written in the treatment of dyes.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow that the cannula of the enema is not received, then you enlarge it with a small iron little by little, and put in there calcium wrapped in Egyptian ointment or in basilikon ointment until it is enlarged.
And similarly if the opening is large, then you gather its lips with a suture and take off from it the quantity in which between the enemas there is an addition.
And if the opening of it from which the breath runs is elevated to its parts above, then dry that in the hollow place from which the breath runs in so that the flesh engendered in there is dried, for when the breath is constricted in the depth of the sinus the flesh is engendered in there.
And if it is not possible for you to dry the sinus within the bone pus base according to how you want, then it is forced that you prepare the limb with a preparation by which the breath runs softly according to what appears to you and that there is not retained in there any cause of the breath.
But that which is over the opening of the sinus, which heals in a whole hour because it dries that which is closed in there of the breath. And so that you know if that which runs from it is motion or a little, or is digested or not digested.
And with that consider the place of that sinus if in it the patient feels pain or not, and if there is an abscess in it.
And when your treatment is prolonged in that manner and you see the matter which is shed over the opening of the sinus already adheres or the adhesion is increased, then loosen it on the third day, and when you loosen the bandage change the ointment.

And if the breath is not digested after several days from the sinus do not know that it does not adhere; and if the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and is prolonged cause, then adjust it in desiccation of the medicines, and if the medicines are moist in their substance and in their strength are dry like a thin dye when the surplus of addition of zegi is adjusted in it.

Of treatment of paronychia and nail abrasion and digit addition and section.
Panaritis is an abscess that develops under the nail of the finger of the hand or foot, and it rarely affects other fingers, and when it is prolonged and its treatment is neglected, there is an abscess of pus, or it becomes corrupt and erode the matrix of the nail, and it strongly affects the entire nail, and the corruption strongly penetrates into the bone within which it has a fetid odor, and the end of the finger becomes rotten, and its color turns brown.
And if you have that which we mentioned in the division and do not find relief from your treatment, then know that you must cut away all the excess that has accumulated under the nail, and then cauterize the wound with hot iron, for cautery is beneficial in such cases.

But a finger that is born to some men is very fleshy, and in it there are few bones, or its nail is very thin.

Of cuts from injuries, these are varicose veins.
Varicose veins are caused by varicose veins, and they are thick wrapped veins full of melancholic humors that come in the members of the body, and their greatest occurrence is in the feet, and especially in the feet of travellers, laborers, and carriers.

Its section is according to this method: at the beginning soak the foot in warm water until the gross blood is dissolved, which is feculent from the foot, and then tighten the foot of the patient at the part above the pus-filled sore of the leg with a bandage, and then dry up the vein in one, or two, or three places with a broad incision, and then elevate the black blood with the palm of the hand from the part below the foot until you have extracted as much as you wish, and as much as the strength of the patient can bear.

On extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in southern lands like Al-Ategi in the land of Arabs and in the southern and arid lands of little tenderness and in which there is no grass nor openings.
And very rarely it is generated in other parts of the body other than the legs.

And when the end appears, then you should wrap around it a piece of lead weighing as much as a drachma, and tie that and leave the lead hanging from the leg.
And as many times as you see some cause to the parts deflating, wrap that in lead and hold it. So that it is drawn out smoothly and is extracted little by little as it is exited from the end of it and does not wind back in the body for any cause.
And from this vein it exits in some men who are in its length of five palms and ten, and I have seen one that exits twenty palms.

On the generation of worms under the skin and it is called bovine disease.
This disease is named in some land because of the cow's disease since it sometimes affects oxen and there is a worm which is born between the vein and the flesh, and goes throughout the body pushing and penetrating, and is shown by its movement from part to part as it ruptures the vein and exits out.
And its generation is through putrefaction of some humors like worms and serpents and earthworms and tapeworms in the belly.

On the generation of disease in children.
This disease is named in our land as children, and it is a pain that comes to some parts, and then it is transferred from part to part.
And you have already seen that according to what you will tell me. He was called to a woman patient in a village, and he discovered her arm and saw a small inflammation in the vein of the arm, and when it was rubbed for an hour, that inflammation rose like silver when it moved from place to place.

And then he showed me that it was revolving through all his body as I saw; and there was great marvel at its change of speed from part to part. And I did not think that it was that because the woman was old, of frail body and veins uncovered, and in her appeared that movement changed.
And it is necessary that it does not appear according to this retro-cure in those who are delicate and with bodies of veins pressed together.

On the extraction of arrows:
Arrows are diverse according to their species and according to the parts of the body they affect: for their diversity is that some have large points, and small, and concave, and blunt, and solid; and among them there are those with three angles, and those with tongues, and those which are stars.
And the signs of the soul are when an arrow strikes it, and pierces the bone, and exits through the hole which is above the brain, for then there occurs violent vomiting, and scotomata, and vertigo, and burning in the eyes, and inflammation and burning of the tongue, and spasms and confusion of the mind and vomiting of bile, and the voice becomes very hoarse and destroyed, and there is a discharge of white moisture from the wound site, and his eyes look like water.
And so if you observe these signs, then refrain from treating the wound and extracting the arrow unless it has exited.
But the signs of the arrow when it strikes the heart are that it is near the left nipple, and feels as though it were lodged in a hard substance, and is not a soft cause; and the movement of the arrow is very similar to the movement of the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and there is chilliness of the extremities, and sweat, and fainting; and so know that death descends quickly.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are sputum of bloody foam from the wound, and the vessels near the neck are swollen, and the color of the patient changes, and he exhales a great spirit with breath, and asks for fresh air to breathe.
And if the arrow strikes the diaphragm which is in the chest, then the pain is near the small ribs, and there is made a great wound with violent pain and with sighing, and not all of the limbs of the muscles are paralyzed.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then quickly see in the wound some undigested food.
And if the arrow strikes the abdomen and is lodged in it, see some rent in the wound, or the membrane, or intestine which is torn, and there is no hope for a cure of him through extracting the arrow.
And if the arrow strikes the bladder and urine exits from it, and some cause processes outward from there to the parts outside, and there is great pain upon the patient, then know that it is mortal.
But the other limbs such as the face, neck, jaw, shoulders, back, ribs, breastbone, thigh, and their likes of the limbs are saved according to the most prominent dispositions when the arrow does not strike a vein or artery and is not a venomous arrow.
And I will recount to you what I have seen of the nature of these arrows so that you may know about your treatment.
The cause is that in a man who had an arrow stuck in the tear duct of his eye, when I extracted it from him through the other side of his nostril, and removed the grays of his eyebrow, he was healed and no harm befell him.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew which had struck him in the grays of his eye, below the eyelid, and the arrow was already concealed and he did not manage to get it except for a small tip with which it adhered to his staff, and it was a great arrow of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with a lion ferrule on which there were no eyebrows.
And the Jew was healed, and no harm befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian which had struck him in the jaw, and it was an Arabic arrow, and it was that to which there were eyebrows; and then I inserted it between two fingers, and it was already submerged in his jaw; and then I made it smaller with that, and then extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had struck him in the belly, and I thought he would die from it; and so when thirty days had passed and there was not changed upon him any cause of his dispositions, I inserted into the arrow and moved it about, and he was healed and no harm befell him.
And I saw a man in whom an arrow had struck the back of his thigh and the wound had healed over him, and after close to seven years, the arrow exited through the root of his thigh.
And I saw a woman in whom an arrow had struck her belly and the wound had healed over her, and she was not changed in her dispositions, and the woman did not suffer harm in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow had struck his face and the wound had healed over him, and the arrow was broken but he did not feel any pain, and similar signs occurred with these.
And I extracted the arrow of the prince of the king's son who had struck him in the bridge of his nose and it had exited through the right side, and the entire arrow was concealed.
And I was called to treat him when the arrow had entered three days prior.
And then I found the wound of the arrow tight, and then I probed it with a subtle probe, and he did not feel that; but he felt a sting below his right eyebrow, and then I sensed that the sting was from the tip of the arrow.
And I applied the spot with ointment in which there is an atractive and digestive virtue, confident that the spot would appear to me as the location of the arrow, so that it exits over it.
And there did not occur in the spot any significant accident that the arrow had struck that spot; and I continued with the ointment over him for several days, and no accident occurred; and the wound healed up in between, and he recovered from the extraction of him after a space of several days until one day he felt the arrow within his nose and showed it to me.
And then I applied to the wound sharp and corrosive medicine for several days until pus opened, and with a probe I probed that, and found the tip of the arrow which adhered to the staff.
And then I applied in the perception of the wound with that sharp medicine until it appeared to me visibly the tip of the arrow, and passed over time with that for the space of four months.
And when the wound has become very inflamed, and it is possible for me to insert forceps into it, and I have cleaned it out, and there is no obstruction preventing its extraction, then I do not cease to manipulate it with clever techniques until, on a particular day, I extract it with specially made forceps, as will be described in the final section of this chapter; and I then repair that wound.
And some doctors believe that the cartilage of the nose cannot be restored, but I have restored it and consolidated the wound; and the patient was completely healed and suffered no ill effects.
And I will now describe the quality of the extraction of certain arrows so that you may understand the rule and significance of what I have not described, for the parts of this art and the definitions of those terms are not covered in this book.
And the skilled surgeon compares a few things about motion, and what is present to what is absent; and he discovers new operations and new instruments through the descent of foreign causes into the art when they occur.
And so I say that arrows are not extracted from the members in which they are lodged, except according to two methods: either by attraction of the place where they enter, or by the opposite of the other part.
And that which is lodged in a fleshy place and is drawn out and extracted is an arrow.
And if it does not obey the extraction in the hour in which it is cut, then you must leave it for a few days until the flesh around it has healed and the attraction and exit of the arrow have been alleviated.
And similarly, if it is lodged in bone and does not obey your extraction, then leave it for a few days and repeat the attraction and motion of it every day until it is extracted.
And if it does not obey your extraction for several days, then you must pierce the circumference of the arrow in that bone with a very fine instrument until the space around the arrow is enlarged and you can then extract it.
But if the arrow is lodged deeply in the body and is hidden from sight, then you must probe it with a probe, and if you feel it, then draw it out with some instruments suitable for attracting it.
And if you cannot reach it because of the constriction of the wound and because of the length of the weapon, and there is no bone or nerve or vein in the way, then pierce the wound until it is enlarged and the arrow can be extracted.
And if there are nails holding it in place, then you must loosen the flesh in which it is lodged completely all around with whatever skill you can; and manipulate it until you can free the flesh from the fracture of the nails and their twisting until it obeys.
And when you administer the extraction of the arrow in whatever place it is, then use with twisting of your hand and forceps on all sides until it obeys; and manipulate with great subtlety so that the arrow is not broken and so that its extraction is not made difficult for you.
And if you cannot extract it in one day, then leave it for a few days until the parts around it have healed, and then repeat the process when it has been alleviated. But if you experience contrary bleeding, then administer what we have said about healing in its chapter.
And guard your skill from cutting into a vein or nerve, or tendon, and manipulate it in every way possible to extract the arrow; and do this with ease and tranquility, and slowness and firmness, just as I have recommended to you and as you have recommended to yourself.
And therefore you should contract the arrow so that the patient is in accordance with the figure according to which the position of the arrow is in him, for it is more beneficial. But if this is not possible for you, then you must administer what is possible for you according to the figures.
But the arrows which are extracted by the opposite of the other part, or which have already entered into some part from outside, or cause hollowness in the extremity of the arrow due to sensitivity of the part above the joint nearest it, then you cut it; and the section should be according to the amount by which the forceps grasp it; and then draw it out, for it is made easier to extract.
But if it is retained in the bone, then you twist with contrary motion until the arrow is impressed into the bone, and widen the hole by yourself, and then extract it.
And if not, leave it for a few days and then repeat the process until it is extracted.
And if the shaft of the arrow is in that, then you push it out with him.

But if the arrow is concave, then you push it out with a strong instrument inserted into it, for the arrow is alleviated by that.

The species of fractures are various, and their figures diverse, and their causes distinct; for some fractures are made by the impact of a spear, and the impact stands all the bones inside the skull, the skull being beneath the bones as it were in a staff, and because of that this species of fracture is named impaction; or the spear pierces through some part of the bone and pierces the flesh around it without breaking into the brainpan, and because of this this species of fracture is called levation absolute.
And the width of these two fractures may be great or small, and they are caused by compression or impact; and the cause of them is impact with a staff or stone, and to their likes.
And this fracture may be penetrating which approaches the membrane beneath the bone or is in the face of the bone, and the width of these two fractures may be great or small.
And of fracture is that it is broken in the bone, in the sottlety of hair small, and because of this this species of fracture is named capillary fracture.
And fracture is that which is made either by impact or by stone and to their likes, which makes some lamina of the bone enter into the spaces between the teeth, and is made in the concave place as it happens to the teeth when they receive impact and is broken into the spaces between the teeth.
