And if you see that accidents are not happening, then you can begin the cure.
The reason for this is that if the patient has come to you in the beginning as it is in the days of winter, then you should study the bone before the 14th day according to all dispositions.
And if it is in the days of summer, then you should cover the bone before the seventh day, so that what is below the bone becomes rotten by the skin, and so that the accidents that we have counted happen.
And if the fracture has already reached the skin, and is in small pieces and crushed, then you should insert the crushed and ground-up part, according to what I will tell you.
And you should cut off the head of the infected patient and uncover it over the bone according to custom and manner for you to make possible according to the shape of the plague, and so that it is lighter on the patient.
And if you see that there is a detection of the bone, flow of blood or pustule cauterized, then you should stop it with what is appropriate, and you should cover the area with cloth soaked in wine and rose oil, and make the plague rest until the pustule is soothed, and be sure to stop the flow of blood; then you may begin on the circuit of the bone and in its removal.
And this should be according to one of two manners of operation.
And the one manner is that you insert the bone with a very narrow incision knife, and this is its shape: And you should also administer another incision knife this one a little bit wider than the other; and this is its shape:
And you should also administer the incision knife that is wider than the second one.
And you should have many incision knives of different widths; some are wider than others, and some are shorter than others and are in the shape of acute angles, and are made of iron or steel.
And use them with ease in striking the incision knife so that it does not strike the head and cause damage to it.
And if the bone is strong and hard, then you should drill into its circuit before you administer the incision knives with drills, which are called non-deep drills.
And they are not named like this but because these drills do not stop the end of the bone from that which is appropriate to it, so that when the drill is round-tipped or edged on its end below the sharp head resembling an edge or a small circle, it prohibits being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take great numbers of these drills, each of which is convenient for the thickness of every bone, so that it is present in all parts of the skull whose end is acute, and the quantity in length and brevity is according to the quantity of thickness of this part.
And this is the shape of three kinds of drills: The quality of drilling in the circuit of the broken bone is that you stop the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers so that you know that the bone has been penetrated; then change the drill to another place, and pause what is between every crack the quantity of the thickness of the ray or environment.
And you should insert with incision knives what is between each crack of the bone, and make that as subtle as possible at the end of lightness, just as we have said, so that you radiate the bone, or by your hand, or by any other instrument which you will have prepared for this, just as tweezers and fine forceps.
And you should keep with great care so that the incision knife does not touch any part of the brain.
And when you have drilled around the bone and it is separated from the brain if it continues with it, then you should extract that, then cut and gnaw at it in its roughness with another instrument similar to an incision knife but however, it should be thicker and more subtle than the other incision knives.
And if any small bones and splinters fall out, then you should pick those up with ease with whatever is convenient for you from the instruments; then you cure the plague with poultices and bandages which we have mentioned.
And the other manner of operation is lighter and more distant from fear, which Gualia says and praises greatly; and this is its speech: It is necessary for you at the beginning to begin uncovering the part of the bone at the site where the fracture is most violent and most manifest, so that when you have uncovered that part and stopped it below with the end of this incision knife, which is long and gentle, and whose shape is as follows [image description]:
And the lenticular part is round and does not insert any cause, and the acute part is from it in two sides forward in length, just as a lever; so that the lenticular part is fitted to the brain with its sharp edge incident on the bone.
And you should percute on the incision knife of one side with a small mallet so that you insert all the bone with ease just as it is turned, and you be sure that the skin does not happen to him in any way which you fear in any manner, and if the patient was the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he was sleeping.
Then if it gives any cause annexed to the bone of the skin in some parts of bone, insert that from it with the end of the lenticular incision knife, and deliver that from it with ease because it is delivered from it without harm and fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the skin, and what is severed or is left of the bone is its edge, and fall out in it roughness and splinters, then you should cut that roughness and gnaw at those splinters with subtle razors which you have prepared their number, of different quantities so that it is convenient for you to administer them in every place the most appropriate of them and the most direct of them according to what you are led by your operation on that broken bone and its shape.
And you should administer in your reason with which you cut the bone at the beginning by the widest of those razors, and then use it also by the most subtle of them, and do not cease to do this according to continuity until you come to administering the thickest and most subtle of all of them.
But in each of the other small capillary fractures and subtle fracture, you should administer cure according to what you are led by rectification of it.
And it is a cause that is not damped on all of which have studied in that art. And I stand on what we wrote, we have shown in great fractures.
And if the skin is stretched when you radiate the bone, then you should take cloth according to the quantity of the plague, and soak it in rose oil, and pause that over the orifice of the plague; then take another cloth folded or doubled, and soak it in wine and rose oil and pause that over the first cloth and do this as gently as possible so that you do not harm the brain; then administer on that a bandage wide, and do not tighten it except by the quantity that the plague has, only.
And let this treatment continue for one or two days until you are sure that the ulcer is cauterized; and then remove it and administer medicines to reduce the swelling, such as flaxseed poultices and linseed meal, and powder of onions and pepper, and similar remedies.
And it is that you make these medicines into a powder that can be sprinkled over the wound, as they are dried; and generally, administer to him all medicine of the nature that cleanses and does not bite.
And be careful according to your ability that the wound in its healing is clean; and do not allow any dirt or other thing on it; nor let virulence accumulate on it; for when it is applied to the membrane of the brain, it corrupts and damages it, and malicious evil occurs upon the patient.
And it happens in the skull, when its bone is cleaned and mainly when the treatment of it is suppressed, and there is blackness on its surface; therefore consider that if these accidents occur to the patient as we have said, know that they are harmful without a doubt.
And if blackness does not occur except from the medicine that is applied on top of it, and it is due to that medicine which causes that darkness, then take honey in a portion and rose oil in portions .iii.. And with those lines, a cloth, and then apply it to the skull; and then heal that with the spices of healing that are appropriate so that he is cured.
Chapter .iii.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two parts only, some of which are more prominent than others; because in it there are two bones, for the part below is cartilage, and it is not broken, and nothing happens to it except crookedness, tortuosity, and sinusitis.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then you must insert your little finger into his nose, and close that fracture within with your index finger, and with your thumb outside, so that the nose returns according to its natural shape. And it must be done by you for ease; and beware of causing pain to the patient due to that operation.
And if the fracture is in the most prominent part of the nose, and the finger cannot reach it, then let it be made equal by the end of the bone in which it is small in thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you must do as follows:
And be careful to restore it on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, according to the rest of the ulcer.
And then insert into the end of the nose a plug of linen cloth if it is one fracture, or two plugs if there are two fractures; and let the thickness of the plug be as much as the amount that fills the crack in the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers say that the plugs should be dipped in honey, and changed every day; but I have not seen it; they say let the plug be dipped in honey comfit with powdered mulberry; and then let it remain so that the bone is firmed and the cartilage becomes hardened.
And sometimes let a plug be inserted into the nose instead of a caliper, made of goose feather wrapped in linen cloth; and it is that when the fracture of the nose is more violent and that the patient is not prohibited from breathing.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you want, do it, and if you want, make calipers.
And if another ulcer occurs, plaster the nose with cerot or cotton saturated in white vinegar and rose oil, or with diaquilon.
But if an ulcer does not occur, then let you plaster that outside with sesamum flour and pepper powdered with honey; and then apply a plug of linen cloth on it, and do not bind the nose with anything for any reason.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted, then split them and extract those with instruments that are appropriate for them; and then sew the cut, and heal it with what flesh-grows and hardens, and with plasters as is convenient.
And if a ulcer occurs inside the nose, then let you heal that with plugs and administer lead tubes so that it is completely healed.
Chapter .iv.
Of the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the jaw is broken and its fracture does not have an ulcer, look to see if the fracture is only outside, and is not broken into two and bent inwards at the ends; then its comprehension is difficult.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, let you insert your left index finger into the mouth of the patient; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert your right index finger; and move it with ease at the ends outside, by your other hand from the part outside of the bone; thus equalize that.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two parts, then let you administer extension of both parts according to rightness so that its equality is possible.
And if teeth occur with disruption, then strengthen those of which you expect that they will be broken by thread of gold or silver, or of silk; and then plaster the broken jaw with cerot, and then place upon it a double cloth, or a large plug decently made, or a weight of only one piece at the length of the jaw; and then tie that on top as you are equipped with its equipment, and as is appropriate for its application, so that it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to keep rest and tranquility, and provide him with food that is soft and not sharp.
And if you estimate that it is already altered in some way, then let you cut and loosen it on the third day; and then rectify what has been altered on it, and plaster it with pepper powder, with honey, or with sesamum flour, after you have removed its cerot, and place upon it wool cloth.
And then after that, when this plaster and adjustment on it is made, and the position of the bone is not altered, let it be left, that is not removed until it is healed and the fracture is strengthened; for often this fracture is healed in three weeks.
And if another ulcer occurs, then administer what we have said several times in the treatment of it so that it is healed, and let the ulcer be licked by that.
And if the fracture has an ulcer, then consider whether it is a broken piece of bone or a splinter, then you must subtilize those splinters with what is appropriate for that in the removal of them.
And if the hole of the ulcer is narrow, then you must widen it by phlebotomy according to the amount of your necessity.
And then after you have extracted those splinters, and if they do not continue to ooze, then let you sew the hole of the ulcer if it is wide; and if not, place upon that one of the plasters which is appropriate for that, so that it is healed.
Chapter .v.
Of the restoration of the collar bone when it is broken.
Many times the furcula of the breastbone is broken in front of the muscle, and that fracture is made in one of three ways.
Because it is either broken, separated, or separated in two parts other than the joints; and it is easier to restore it; or it happens that there are injuries to the joints, and it is difficult to restore it; or that the fracture is a plague.
And the operation on that, when the fracture is the plague, is that you have two ministers present, so that one has the assistant and follows the broken furcula, and the other extends the neck towards that part.
Then fix the fracture with the threads within so that it is made according to its shape and there is neither prominence nor concavity.
And if it needs more extension, then you have to put under the patient's chin a sponge or woolen ball, and the size of it according to the amount of your need; and extend and elevate the furcula and tighten the sponge with your hand within so that you fix the fracture according to its shape.
And if you cannot pull the end of the furcula to the outside parts because it is reached by the prenon, then you have to make the patient recline on his neck, and put under the muscle a cushion half in size.
And the ministers press the muscle from the outside parts under so that the bone of the furcula is elevated which is in the prenon above, and then you straighten the fracture and fix it with the threads.
But if you feel injuries of the furcula and bleeding, then you have to cut on that and pull out those injuries with ease.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you devise an incision of it with one of the saws which you have prepared for that, after you have put under the furcula the instrument that serves as a vise; and this instrument is of wood or iron and this is its shape:
And it is similar to a spoon whose concavity is like this; and its breadth is according to what you need from it, according to the size of the bone or the thickness of it.
But its length is according to what is possible for you in the operation; that it has two ends like this, and one end is wide and the other narrow.
And if the incision of the plague which you have cut when you have pulled out the injury of the bone is wide, and there is a risk of an abscess, then you join the two lips of the plague with a suture.
And if the disruption is little, or you fear an abscess, then you fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the quantity of the incision of the wound.
And if there is an abscess, then you soak a cloth in warm wine and oil and place it over that.
And all that it is not bleeding over the broken bone; and so you have to see the patient every day, that when the binding is softened or you see the stake is already bleeding, then you straighten that and strengthen the binding.
And then place the pillow of the patient under his neck, and place under his chin from below the pillow in the night a little cushion; so that with that is elevated the support of it of the side of that, and therefore is elevated the fracture of the furcula to elevation of the muscle; or be the arm of it tied to its neck.
And do not untie the binding unless there is sufficient healing or abscess within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you have seen its face to that, and tighten in the compression and restore that and leave it until it is restored and the fracture of the furcula is coagulated.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the month in twenty-eight days, and some times in some men is done in less time of that.
Chapter .VI.
Of restoration of fracture of the shoulder.
The spatula is broken in its lateral place and not broken from it except its margins.
And therefore when it is broken in its place — and it is broken in the middle of it — and it is not connected except by contact, then according to the figure of the fracture you make the preparation of it and reduce that according to the natural figure in all manner of engine which is to you possible.
And then place on the place powder of wheat with egg white and piece of linen; and then place doubled cloth; and then place over it stake bent of thin table according to the quantity of the spatula all or more little.
But if under the stake is concavity in some of the places of the spatula, then you fix that concavity with piece of linen, and then position the stake according to equality.
And then strengthen above with a long bandage with decent compression and with firmness of the stake so that it is not bleeding from its place.
And consider the binding every day; and all times that it is softened, bind another time and tighten it and adjust the stake if it is bleeding from its place.
And watch over the patient on his side.
And the spatula is restored in twenty days or in .XXV.
And therefore when it is completed that number, then you dissolve the binding, and so you know assuredly that that is of the bones of which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if process of the place of the bone weighs and put under it the thread, then you cut on it, and support it, and do according to what we have said in the fracture of the furcula of fear of abscess if it happens here.
Chapter .VII.
Of restoration of fracture of the foot.
The feet sometimes are broken in their middle and that usually inside; but the ends of it most often that they are not healed and crushed.
And of the accidents of it when it is broken in its middle it declines to the parts inside and there happens to it great pain, and difficulty in walking, and coughs, and fever, and vomits blood; and it is declared in the cavity in the broken bone and it does not hide from feeling.
And its restoration is that the patient reclines on his back, and place between the shoulders of it a cushion; and then press the muscles of it, and adjust the ribs of it with the hands of two cost, and subtilize in the operation of it and in the adjustment according to all manner which is to you possible and that convenes to that, so that return the figure of the bone according as it is said.
And then place a plaster and a bandage on the patient, and place a thin board made of poplar or willow, or something similar in shape, on top of it; and then wrap that in a cloth; and gently tie that to the broken bone with a thin string; and bind that tightly around the back several times, and tighten it decently; and consider the binding at all times, and when it is loosened, tighten it.
And if the need arises to remove the bandage because of any redness or inflammation, or pain, or abscess that occurs in the area, then you should cut and loosen it, and remove the plaster, and correct what has occurred through the manner of its treatment; and then return the plaster, once the face has healed; and adjust the binding so that it heals.
Chapter VIII.
Of the restoration of broken bones.
Know that fractures of the ribs occur only in large places that follow the back; and of their ends, in front, they do not occur to those except through what is cartilaginous. And its knowledge is not concealed from the feeling due to the inquiry of it with the fingers.
And the restoration should be in such a way that it joins the fracture with the fingers according to the possible manner, as long as it is swallowed according to how it covers, and then plaster and tighten the broken bone with a bandage, if needed for that.
And if the fracture of the ribs is declining to the inside parts, then it occurs in the patient violent pain and piercing, as well as piercing that occurs in him who has pleurisy, and due to that which puts the membrane on the bone; and it occurs in him difficulty of breathing, and coughing, and vomiting blood; and this one is of difficult cure.
And already the antibiotics are born in that with many ingredients.
And of them I say: that you place poultices on the patient who engenders inflammation and swelling, so that the belly is inflated and extended, and the fracture is pushed to the outside parts.
But let us first eat that so that it is not a lesser cause to generate an abscess with cautery, if it does not occur; and if it already occurs, then let it help with that.
And some of them say that a poultice should be placed above and that it should suck strongly; and it is like reasoning, but nevertheless it is feared that the poultice throws the superfluities to the place due to weakness.
And some of them say: that the place should be covered with wool that has already been submerged in hot oil, and that there are placed powders between the ribs so that it is filled, and that the binding is tied as you wrap that according to roundness; and let the patient be treated for pleurisy with poultice and medicine.
And if it causes violent constriction in the patient who does not suffer, and the bone pushes the membrane with piercing pain, then fear for the patient, and let there be cut on the spot, discovered of the broken rib, and then place over that instrument that conserves the cipack, the remembrance of which is preceded, and that we pierce the bone with ease, and extract it.
And then let us sew together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a stitch, and heal that with plasters as long as it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs inside, then cut and put powders in rose oil and place it over the spot, and let the patient be treated for that with what covers the inside of the abscess.
And let him lie on his side over which he sleeps, as long as he is healed.
Chapter IX.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the spine of the back and of the neck.
Sometimes it is a fracture in the bones of the neck, and sometimes it affects them with attrition, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And when it occurs in someone and you want to know whether he will be healed or not, then consider that if his fingers are already softened and stupified and dead and cannot move them nor extend them nor tighten them, and when you pinch them with your fingers or pierce them with a needle and do not feel pain in them, then know that it is not healed, and in the same of dispositions it is mortal.
And if they move and feel constriction in them with the fingers and piercing, then know that the joint of the bone is saved, and that the patient will heal with treatment.
And if you see the vertebrae of the neck like that and want to know whether it will be healed or not, then consider his feet.
Then if you see that they are already softened and that occurs in them what occurred in the fingers, and then when he lies on his back, he has windiness and ejaculation against his will, and sometimes he lies on his back and wants to urinate but cannot, then know that it is mortal, and therefore you should not treat him.
But if none of those occur, the cause is better and less distant.
And the treatment of him is that you persevere in the application of the poultice with cautery which you place over the atrissat vertebra, with oil of rose alone, or with seeds of wild onions; place it on that some times a day as long as the abscess cautery is sedated.
And then place one of the comforting and exciting plasters, and tighten that with a bandage; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and not to sleep on the side that causes him pain as long as he is healed.
And if it occurs due to the attrition of the bone or any other cause that is already separated from him, then cut above the tendon and let them tie up that bone; and then sew together the two lips of the wound with a stitch if it is large; and then heal that with some embrocation of the gums, as long as it is healed.
But if it is broken the end of the bone of the shoulder blade, and it is the end of the coat, then let you insert the tip of the left hand into the hole, and join the broken bone with the other hand according to what is possible by second apposition; and then place a plaster and splint over that, if needed for them, and then tighten that.
And if you feel broken splinters in him, then open that and tie up that bone, and heal the wound with the treatment that has been preceded as long as it is healed.
Chapter X.
Of the restoration of the shoulder.
The bones of the shoulders often become broken, and if they are broken, then the fracture of them is only a separation in their ends and they are inserted into the length and decline to the inside parts; and it occurs in the patient violent pain in the spot and piercing, and the shoulder is stupified when it is broken.
And its restoration is that you extend your hand over that as long as it is over the fracture, which is the figure of it.
And if the fracture is only in its end, then you join that according to what appears to you by second apposition as long as it is assembled into its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in the length or what already declines to the inside parts, then let the patient lie on his belly as long as you prepare for its restoration of that fracture. And therefore when you have joined that, place a plaster over it, and then place over that a splint of wood or of tendon, and tighten that by which you do not fear piercing of fracture nor removal of the splint.
And fill the cavity with what fills it according to its capacity, and instruct the patient to sleep on his back or on his side.
But if an abscess occurs, then cease from the extension of it and its restoration until the abscess is suppurated, and place upon it that which dries it according to what has been preceded; and return to the restoration of it, and tighten it thus with a bandage.
And if there occur in the bones cracks or they are crushed, then you treat them.
Chapter 11.
On the restoration of a fracture of the elbow.
The elbow is that which is between the cuff and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is fractured, then its restoration is in one of two ways, one of which is that you take a plaster of moderate thickness according to this form:
And there should be tied at both ends two ligaments, and it should be suspended from the elevated place.
And let the patient follow on a chair; and then bend out his fractured arm over the beam until it reaches the tip of it in the middle of which is attached the curve of the beam.
And let it be suspended over some heavy object, or the assistant extend the arm to its limits; and then let the doctor and his assistants press the fracture with their hands, until the fracture returns as it should.
And the other way is that the patient be reclined on his neck and suspend his arm by a band from around his neck; and then instruct two ministers, one of whom has what is above the fracture with his hands, and the other has what is below him; each of them extending to his limit.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then tighten with a band over the fracture and another over that, and each of the two ministers extend to their limits.
And therefore if the fracture is near the end of the bone, then it must be placed half of the band over the tip and the other below the fracture under the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, then it must be placed over that location and over the elbow.
Gently apply it without pain and then when it is well applied, tighten it if an abscess does not occur.
But if an abscess occurs, then let the constriction cease within seven days, and place over it fine wool soaked in wine and rose oil until the abscess drains, and then tighten it.
And the way to tighten it is that you place plaster over the fracture and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then bend the arm over that elbow, and place your open hand over his bicep.
And place the cloth and tighten it over the elbow so that the arm is in a stellate position, unless prohibited by some cause of the fractured bone.
And then use splints.
And it is that you place over the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers in total.
And then tighten over the splints with that constriction which has been stated at the beginning of this chapter.
And it is that your constriction over the site of the fracture be tighter. And when the fracture is more elongated, then the constriction is less.
And therefore if you see the position of the splints and the constriction as we have said in the time in which you restore the limb to its setting, then do it.
And if you fear an abscess, then let the constriction and splints cease on the seventh day as we have said.
And then consider the band in all parts for three days so that no abscess occurs at the site or inflammation; or it is prohibited for food to come to the limb due to disposition of excess constriction; and then rectify all that as we have said.
But if you are sure that it does not occur due to that, then do not remove the bandage except after three days or more.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and let his arms be over his stomach.
And place over the elbow a poultice of wool soaked in water, and consider throughout the night or day that it is not looser nor softer, or that the band is柔軟ized; then rectify all with your skill.
And place the patient's food according to the order which we have given before, so that it is subtle at the beginning until it is started to be tightened; and then let it be thicker for him.
The habit of the elbow and his arm is that they are tight for forty days; and then let them be looser and a bath be administered to him and he be anointed with ointments which rectify it.
And if the fracture is healed, then you do not remove from that the band and splints until ten days or two months.
Chapter 12.
On the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
An arm is made up of two bones which are called two bones; one of which is small, and it is that which pushes the finger, and the other is large and is that which is placed below the small one, from the part more below.
And usually it is only fractured the larger bone, or the smaller, and rarely are they both fractured together.
And when the small bone is fractured from above, then its restoration is easier, and its healing is easier.
And when it is fractured below, then the fracture of it is bad, and its healing is difficult.
Worse than that is when both bones are fractured together.
Therefore if the bone which is fractured is the small one from above, then it is convenient for the doctor, in order to restore it, that he apply a slight extension with ease so that it is soaked.
But if the large bone is that which is fractured, then it must be applied a stronger extension.
And if both bones are fractured together, then it must be applied the strongest extension.
And let the figure of the hand be placed in order to restore it and the extension of it be extended over a pillow, and the wrist of the hand to the parts above, more out than all the others fingers, and the little finger be more down than the others fingers, and the patient be recumbent on a raised pillow.
And let one pillow be before him in his height or sideways from him so that the patient does not struggle too much.
And then let the assistant extend the arm from the part more below with his hand or with a band, and another assistant extend over it similarly.
And then let the doctor soak the bone so that it is reduced according to the best figure which he can.
And if in the forming of the bone there are bumps, then thoroughly reduce all pressure on it with your skill.
And if it appears in him separated that it pierces the tendon and you do not have hope in its healing, then stop it and treat it according to the way which we have said over it.
But if the fracture is with pus, I have already done in that chapter singular, and therefore take the cure of it.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then you should line a cloth with ceratum made from rose oil and white wax, making it medium between thin and thick, and tightly wind the cloth around that abscess until it subsides; then remove the ceratum and apply a plaster prepared from flour with egg white, and then apply leaves; let the leaf which is applied over that fracture be more moist and stronger.
And know that the number of leaves on the arm varies according to different dispositions, or whether the fracture is in one or two places together.
Then apply your ligature over the site of the fracture more strongly and more aggressively, and as the ligature loosens at the ends above the fracture or at the lower ends of him, loosen the ligature a little bit according to his memory in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moistened, and not hard or rough, and the thread with which it is tied should be medium between thin and thick, as we have recommended.
And consider the member and its ligament for a few days. And if there is any cause why rectification is necessary, as you find out about the member, then you should bathe the member with warm water until that discomfort ceases.
And let the member not be constricted while resting, and then return the ligature.
And if the ligature is already loosened and the bone is already healed and like those, then rectify all that with your skill.
And consider more if the nourishment is prohibited from coming to the member due to excess ligature, then loosen that a little and let it be for a few days until that nourishment runs to that, and then tighten that.
But if there is no cause in the patient of those which we have said, then it should not be loosened for about 20 days or so.
And then the patient's hand should be suspended by his skill of agitated movement, and let him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or around 32 days, or rather it is restored in 28 days, and all that is according to the disposition of the complexities and dispositions of skill.
Chapter 13.
Of the restoration of fracture of the thumb and fingers.

Chapter 14.
Of the restoration of fracture of the bone of the thigh.
The bone of the thigh many times is broken and is declared to feeling according to how it is converted to the front party and to the most upper.

But if it is near the root of the thigh, then let the ligature be near the hollow to the part of the pencil, so that the extension box in them, and the other ligature below the fracture.
And likewise if the break is near the hollow, then let the ligature be made near the hollow, and let the extension be to the lower parts.
And then the doctor greases the bone with both his hands within so that he reduces it according to the natural resemblance and the bone is joined by good junction; then let him apply plaster and ligature if there is no abscess in the member. But if there happens to him abscess, then you leave that for a few days until the abscess is subsided, and then return to your cure.
The ligature of it is done thus: cover over the fracture with a hard bandage and fold that two or three times, and loosen from him superfluous; then wrap the thigh so that it comes to the ankle and the root of the thigh.

And then loosen the ligature over that, when there happens to the member corrosion nor abscess nor inflammation and likeness to those; and if there is any cause of those, then know you with loosening from him, and rectify all that happens to him altogether, as we have said many times.
But if there is pain in the bone and it becomes infected, then you must have it lanced as much as possible; if not, cut over it and let it drain, and heal with that which has been preceded by remembrance until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is made strong by means of splints, as we have said in the case of the arm and the shoulder.

Chapter .xv.

Chapter .xvi.
Of the restoration of a broken thigh.
Know that in the thigh there are two bones of which one is thick and is called by the name of the thigh; and the other is thin and is called the fibula.
And happens to each of the kinds of fracture what happens to both bones of the arm, and for this the restoration of it is done in the same way as the restoration of the arm lanced, and the operation on it is one and the same.
And if both bones are split apart, then the thigh is broken into all parts.
And if the thinner bone is split, then the thigh is broken towards the front.
And if the thicker bone is split, then it is caused by those things which are not made by you.
Then administer extension and cautery and ligation of the splinters according to that lancing.

And a brace of wood has been administered according to the length of the thigh; and let it be paused on it so that it conserves that from movement.

Chapter .xvii.
Of the fracture of the heel and of the bones of the leg and of the toes.
To the heel does not happen fracture altogether; but to the bones of the leg happens fracture sometimes; and to the toes of the leg happens fracture scarcely, and only happens to them a sprain in the months of disposition.

Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of fractured bones when they are swollen.
It is convenient for him to whom the fracture occurs with swelling, and especially if the bone is large like that of the thigh or of the buttock, or similar to it, that you treat and flebotomize him immediately if the conditions of flebotomia testify so as we have said above.
And if the swelling flows blood, then know that you treat its retention in the same way as you powder it with powdered zinc, unless another cause is present.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that very day, and do not prolong it, if apostema does not occur.
But if the apostema becomes cauterized, then let you leave off its restoration until the ninth day, while the apostema subsides, and do not approach it on the third day and the fourth of all, because you will cause malignant accidents.
But if the fractured bone is prominent above the uncovered tendon, then know that you must pierce it to reduce and smooth it with your hands with ease and with little extension.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the equation of it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands with this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, the length of which is seven or eight digits and the width of which is according to the amount of swelling; and it is necessary for the doctor to take these three or four according to the amount that he requires in the cure in every kind of fracture.
And it is rounded and has a little thickness so that it is not bent by the pressure upon it during the hour of the operation.
And its upper part has an acute end which is beveled at the edges; and its underside is inclined, and it is thicker in the middle and inclines towards the ends.
And this is its form:
And approach the instrument to the swelling so that you place its acute end over the fractured bone with ease. And it is named in Greek "labre volentz atelarem", so it is a little palette.
So that its acute end be placed elevated over the prominent end of the bone.
And press it in a wayward manner so that the bone returns, and let it be smoothed with some equation.
And therefore press you to smooth the ends of the fracture between them, and if the end of the broken bone is thin, and do not take that instrument by good approximation, then know that you must insert the end of that bone so that the instrument has power over it.

And then rub that which is rough and has small scars.
And when the bone is reduced and moreover the reduction causes the patient intense pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and therefore do what you confer with the patient great benefit.
When you have completed the restoration which you do to the bone, then immerse cloth in black astringent wine, and properly if it is in summer, and do not place cerot or any other cause over the swelling so that no putrefaction and corruption occur in it.
And then administer stelas in the hour of your completion of the restoration of the bone, and leave the swelling uncovered so that you may insert the wound with tweezers and use bandages according to the amount of swelling.
And guard yourself with all caution so that you do not strain the swelling with the fracture, because many times they have done this the foolish doctors and caused over their patients death or a malignant corrosion or putrefaction, and it is so harmful. And if your stricture is loose, soft, different from the stricture of other fractures.
And if the swelling is great or is bad, and you fear over it some harmful accidents which we have recommended, and there is pain in the place, then know that you must not pause over it astelas, but do to that an envelope of hard cloths instead of astelas and tighten it with those.
And when a day or two has passed since the swelling began and there is engorgement in it, then you must place over it the cloth with which you covered it with wine, and then administer hot waters, with which usually doctors medicine swellings, thus with tetrafarmacon and similarly.
And it seems to you that you loosen the ligature and consider the swelling at dawn and evening until it is consolidated and closed up, and know that the member be prepared in such a way that the putrefaction flows from it to the ends with ease.
If the swelling passes several days and is not consolidated or is not abscinded from it the engorgement, then know that here you are at risk of the small pains for which you must treat the swelling with tenting. And of those small pains which are separated and extracted, those from which the member is not separated and they cause pain, pierce them according to what you can.
And if it happens that the plague becomes swollen or bright red, then it is stinging nettle, or another species of corruption and putrefaction, then you must apply to each one of them what suits them from the cure, of which the narrative precedes in their own chapter.
And in which you must stand firm with your speech and make your request: when the leg is broken, it protrudes over the limb just like the bone of the thigh and the helper, and similar to those of the large limbs, and do not assume attraction from it, nor extraction of it for many times death happens through that; have the patient lie on his side so that he can rest, for often he will fall on his own after about 20 or 30 days. And then cure the plague if you have seen it, and if not leave it alone.
Chapter .20.
Of the cure for nodules that happen on the path of a fracture.
Many times this nodule happens on the path of healing from a fracture, and most often it is near the joints, and by it the shape of the limb is distorted; and very soon the limb is prohibited from its natural operation.
Therefore consider if the nodule is recent and administer to it medicines that have astringent properties like aloe, and thistle, and marjoram, and acacia, and similar to these; so that you take from those some or all of them, and mix those with white wine, or sour wine, or sweet wine, and apply that to the nodule on a piece of cloth, and constrict that around it with a good constriction.
And let the constriction stay on for many days so that it does not come undone for several days; and then loosen it and repeat another, while the nodule is being softened; or press a sheet of lead gently on top, because lead has the property of softening anything swollen in the limbs.
But if the nodule is already converted into stone and it is tight and necessitates being cut open, then split that through its uppermost part, and excise the excess and prominent part, or pierce that with some sharp instrument so that it is softened; and cure that which has been cut.
Chapter .21.
Of the restoration of a fracture when it is restored and then the limb becomes weak and frail not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is restored, and then the limb becomes weak and frail, that is not done except for many causes: of which one cause is the abundance of soluation of the ligament and of the binding of it done not according to how it should be; or by excess of constriction of the ligaments so that nourishment is prohibited from running to the limb; or by abundance of superfluous embrocations; or by superfluous movement not at the right time; or by lack of blood in the body of the patient and by its debility.
And the cure for that is to make the patient eat and to make his heart tender so that blood is multiplied in him; and administer a bath; and apply oil and lettuce over it.
And then apply poultice over the limb so that the poultice brings many nutrients to it, or continue embrocation of it with warm water so that nourishment runs and returns to its natural figure.
Chapter .22.
Of the cure of broken bones when they are restored crookedly and prohibited from their operation of what is right.
When it happens to the restored limb the healing of crookedness and eminence at the broken bone and nodule, and it is fed up with the form of the limb, but the limb is not prohibited from its natural operation, then do not take the word of the one who examines that limb as though it is newly broken.
But there are already many foolish doctors and restoratives who have done that in our land; and this operation is very blameworthy presenting great fear, the sign of which is deadly.
But if the claudication and nodule are recent, then let them be embrocated with warm water in which soft herbs are steeped, like fennel and thistle roots, and marjoram, and similar to these; and let an ointment be made from a mild emulsion just like dill oil; or let it be plied with mallow juice and let it be bound up with linen, or with cotton, or with silk; or let it be bound with fat figures, and bound with dove's droppings, and similar to these of the medicines that are named consolida

Chapter .23.
Of speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is dislocation of one of the joints of its place so that it is prohibited from motion, and the figure of the limb is distorted, and it brings upon the patient great pains and injuries.
Therefore when dislocation happens, then let it be reduced immediately, and not delay that reduction, for if it is delayed the joint is apostoamated and very soon with that dislocation is made difficult to reduce.
And for that reason let it not be struck nor stretched in the hour of apostoamatio, because many times it brings on spasms and pains to the patient.
But when that happens, then let you pay attention to the apostoamatio of the patient, and then let it be left alone so that the apostoamatio rests; and then let the limb be embrocated with warm water and oil, and then let it be reduced gently.
And let every limb be cured by that of which the memory will come in its place.
And now we have ordered the chapters of dislocation according to how it is preceded in fracture, from the uppermost part of the body down to the lowermost part.
Chapter .24.
Of the cure for dislocation of the lower jaw.
So dislocated are the two jaws if not tight; and the dislocation of both is done according to one of two ways: they are pulled from their places by small movement, and are softened a little; or they are dislocated by entire dislocation and completed, so that they are softened at the joint where saliva runs from the patient's mouth and he cannot hold it closed, and his tongue is wrapped in his speech.
But when its dislocation is small, then in the major dispositions it returns by itself for no reason.
But if the dislocation is entire and completed, then let the reduction of it be administered gently, and not be delayed at all.
And let the minister hold the patient's head, and let the doctor insert his one hand's finger into the root of the dislocation of the jaw inside the mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or let him insert both hands simultaneously if the dislocation is on two sides, and the other fingers of his hands be outside holding them; and command the patient to stop clenching his jaws and open those at every part; and the doctor grip the jaw and press it so that it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both mandibles are dislocated, then administer an evaporator with water and oil until both are reduced and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And when they are returned and bitten, and the mouth of the patient is closed and not relieved, then place a powder of cloth with cerot over them, made of wax and rosin oil; and then let it be bound gently with soft ligatures.

And this should be administered gently, and do not work on the opening of the mouth after eating and drinking and resting, until the mandible is swollen and healed.
And if the reduction of both mandibles is difficult when they are dislocated at any time and are not returned to their places, then many times afterward there occur feverish heat and constant vomiting, and very soon the patient's belly is inflated, and very soon he vomits pure bile.

Chapter 25.
On the reduction of dislocation of the furcula and the extremity of the muscle.

Chapter 26.
On the reduction of dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is dislocated only in three ways, of which one is that it is dislocated at the top of the joint at the lower end.
And the second is that it is dislocated at the top of the muscle, and this occurs rarely; and it is not dislocated at the sides behind the shoulder; and it is not dislocated at the front by the nerves.
And most commonly it is dislocated and protrudes outward at the lower end below the joint, especially in those whose flesh is thin; but in those who have much flesh, it is protruded outward with difficulty.

Chapter .XXVII.
Of healing of dislocation of the collarbone.
Know that the joint of the collarbone is dislocated with difficulty and returns likewise with difficulty; and it is dislocated to all sides, and most to the front side, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because when you adjust the dislocated collarbone with your hand, that is made manifest to you, for the joint is hollow and the arm cannot be bent, nor can it be touched with that one's own muscle.
And that which is dislocated to the sides and you reduce the dislocation as soon as it comes to an abscess, for if it comes to an abscess, its reduction is difficult, and often it is not healed at all; and especially when it is to the back side, for it is the worst of all kinds of dislocations, and of greater pain, and many times death ensues from it.
And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the hand of the patient be stretched with two hands, and the arm of the patient be expanded, and the hands of the doctor are on the collarbone and under it, and he presses the joint with the tips of his fingers or with the root of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is to the front side, then it returns as he bends his hand with his hand, while he strikes the collarbone with the root of his palm.
And if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer violent extension and great motion.
And it is that two ministers extend the arm, and they hold that joint in such a way that it is not moved because of the extension.
And then the arm is to be rolled all around, as it is wrapped with folded cloth over the hand of the patient, or ligament.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, he anoints that with some oil in order that it be made smooth with facility; and then he presses the joint with violent impulse until it returns.
And then its reduction should be stopped over that plaster in which it is stiffened and dried with egg white, and it is stretched with descending compression, and the arm is suspended from the neck, and is left for several days.
And then it is released; and if the joint is formed in its place, then you release the ligament of it and leave that.
And if you see the joint is not stronger, then you repeat the plaster and the ligament, and leave that for several days until it is stretched out; and then release that.
But if it happens to it difficulty with the reduction of the joint and its disposition is in movement, then administer humection in a bath and subtle friction, and compression, until it is relieved; or pause over the joint all-healed, as it is a poultice of soft fat; and then release that, and leave it for the day and night. And then release that one, and put that in a bath; and when he rises, then you rub the joint with tempered friction, and then repeat over that all-healed, as it is a liniment of oil, once a day, and the second, and the third. Corruption has already occurred to the brain due to the heat of the bath while it was wet.
And if you want, pause over that split of wet and hot cow's udder over it; and do that several times, for that is wet and returns to its disposition.
Chapter .XXVIII.
Of healing of dislocation of the ulna, which is of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
The hand sometimes is dislocated, and the reduction of its dislocation is easier, and the contrary is of the other joints, but however if there is swiftness in the reduction of it in the hour in which it is dislocated, before that the place becomes an abscess or an abscess occurs in it.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you stop the joint of the hand of the patient on a table and the minister extends the hand of that one, and the doctor stops his hand or his palm over the prominence of the joint, and he presses that until it returns to its place.
But however if it is considered that the palm is already struck to the belly of the hand, then the patient stops his two hands on a table because of extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is before the two of the hand, then let the position of the hand of that be intriniceamment over the table, so that the hand of the doctor boxes over that prominence of the joint.
And then if it returns at once, good; and if not, then strengthen that with plaster yielding the abscess and leave that; and do not repeat that, for if you do, you will not endure nor be able to reduce it further than you pass over it days.
But however the joint is bent according to its tortuousness; and if the patient feels no cause but that the hand is softened and cannot be strengthened by any cause, then know that the nerves are cut or torn, and then there is no power in it except that it is tightened with cautery close to the break of it and strongly indeed that confers any cause.
Then when the ulna is returned, then stop over it that plaster which we have recommended, and let it be left and tightened for five days; and then release that and let the hand of him be exercised.
But if its movement is hindered and some difficulty occurs in it, then loosen that with lukewarm water and with friction several times, until it is relieved.
Chapter .XXIX.
Of the healing of dislocation of the phalanges.
Sometimes the phalanges are dislocated all the way around.
And when any phalanx of those is dislocated to the back of the palm or to its belly, then stop the phalanx and push the dislocation of it with your fingers, until it returns.
And support the head of the finger, suspend that which is dislocated on both sides, and leave it for two days.
And then extend the dislocated part and straighten it on the same day.
And when night comes, take that second part according to the narrative, and do not cease from loosening it during the day, and exercise with movement, and do so for several days until it is stretched out straight.
And do the same if it is dislocated in the belly of the hand; take that part and do to it your first operation, until it is healed.
And likewise do for the other dislocated parts.
Chapter 30
Of the cure of dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation occurs to any of the vertebrae of the back or neck, and they are completely torn from their sockets, then in that case there is no cure, for death seizes the patient.
And the sign of this is that the ejection of the patient is outside of his will, and he cannot retain it; and many times some members of him are made soft, or two legs of him, or two arms of him, or one of those; or a vertebra is torn from its socket, for many times the removal of these is made to four parts.
Therefore it is torn from the back parts and is named dislocation.
But the cure for this is that you should consider if the dislocation occurred due to fragility, because in that case there is no cure and there is no healing at all.
But for that which comes from falling or hitting or similarly to those, the Ancients have already spoken of the methods of cure with a prolix speech, of which the most returns by profit; but I and briefly from that of which the few excuse the excess of it, by my proximity to my own intention and exposition, by which I have here exposed and formed from diverse instruments, by which I will show and space out that.
And of those there is no cure except for that which occurs specifically to the back with what I will now count.
And that the patient be extended on his face close to a wall, and be extended under him instrument wet so that he is not tied at the feet.
And then a straight stick be placed, fixt in the earth at the end of the bed around the head of him, and another stick in a line that extends his feet in the other extremity of the bed.
And the minister hold the stick, and it should not be fixed in a hole; and another minister hold the other second in that form.
And then wrap around the head of the patient and let the teeth of the lion clip under him; and extend the end of the clip to the stick which is supposed to be the head of him, and take that on him; and then tighten all the clips and take those on the other stick which is supposed to be the feet of him.
And then each minister extend the clip to the end of the stick, and let them not be torn from the two sticks and the clips of their sockets in which they are fixt, but on the contrary do not be fixed as I have said.
And the doctor place his palms on the vertebra with strength within until he returns, or that he be placed over it a table, and then that he adjust his feet over the table within until he returns.
But if he does not return by this form, then take a table of which the breadth or length is in line with three cubits, and dig in the wall from which we have said that it is proper to the patient a place in which to put between the end of the table. And then place the middle of the table on the vertebra, and the doctor place his feet over the other end, and tighten be within until the vertebra is bound and returns to its socket.
And if you wish, open with a turn which is wound around the hand; and that you fix to the earth under the head of the patient at the end of the bed two sticks, the length of which is one cubit, and fix the head of him in the end of the bed winding two sticks; and let the length be between each stick one palm, and now it is made in each stick a hole in which to run torn, and each be set firm in earth so that it is not torn.
And let there be a round stick in which is the turn, and let it be wound around the two holes of the two sticks.
And at the end of it is a hole in which is formed the stick, the length of which is one palm with that which is wound around; and likewise in the other two sticks.
And then tighten the clips which you have tightened in the feet of the patient in the hole which is supposed to be the head of him, and the clips which you have tightened in his thighs in the turn which is supposed to be the feet of him.
And then each turn and stand a minister who twists the turn with his hand, and the doctor bends the vertebra according to what we have said.
And then when the vertebra is returned and the socket bent, then cover that you stop exciccatum plaster with oil, and then stop; and then pause over the plaster a board of which the breadth is three digits or around those, and the length of it is quantity that takes the place of the dislocation, and over the part of the vertebra.
And then let him be tied by the clip which we have said, and use the patient of subtle nourishment within until he is healed.
And if any swelling remains in the socket at the end of the healing, then cover that they be administered medicines that moisten and loosen by administration of the board for a long time, which we have recounted; and now is administered in that socket sheet of lead.
And sometimes swelling occurs in the back of the neck, and it is caused by dislocation; and it is that bone which now swells so that it does not cover that be made projection on him with this medicine, for he will surely cause death to you.
Chapter 31
Of the cure of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle do not end only dislocation, and does not occur to those that occurs to other joints of small removal and concavity.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated according to four ways, for it is dislocated to the parties within, or to the parties without, or to the parties before, or to the parties behind.
And most commonly it is dislocated to the parties within, and seldom to the parties before or to the parties behind.
And the sign of its dislocation to the parties within is that when you bend the hip of the patient who has healed with the patient, it is longer and the joint is more prominent than that which has healed; and the patient cannot bend his foot because of the stretching, and it is the socket that the stretching has made apotemized from apothema cauterized, so that the head of the hip has been drawn here.
The sign of that person to whom dislocation happens is that the parts are pushed out, it is that the accidents of him are contrary to this accident.
The sign of that person to whom dislocation happens in front is that he expands his chest according to the compliment, but above all does not bend that part which is painful, and if he tries to go forward and cannot, and his urine is retained, and his testicles are swollen, and he must walk on tiptoe.
The sign of that person to whom dislocation happens behind is that he does not expand the bottom, or cannot bend that part which bends the testicles, and his chest is shorter than the other, and the testicles are softened, and the head of the thigh is drawn towards that place.
But the reduction of this dislocation is that you consider, according to time, if the dislocation is old and ancient, and has been long established with you, administering its reduction and remaining according to its disposition, then it will not be completely cured; and therefore no compression should be used upon it.
But with the person who has the recent dislocation, and it is one of the four kinds of dislocations, then you should return to that which returns the joint and extend it to the parts within and without, and alternately to the right and left, for it quickly returns and does not require any other cure.
But if it does not return in this manner, then you should prepare to extend the chest of him from the lower part, or with his own hands, or with a bandage which is tied above the chest; and another extends the hand of that one above the navel, so that he inserts his hands under the nipples of him; and another tightens with a bandage the root of the thigh, and holds the other end of the bandage.
And if you see that the dislocation is on the chest from the end of the chest, then you should cauterize that which is under the nipple of the breast; and it should be made to extend with a bandage of twisted wool from the cautery on the chest from the end of the bandage.
And it should be the extension of all those in one direction, so that the patient is elevated by his body from the earth and he remains hanging.
And this species of extension is common to all four.
And therefore if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then it is necessary in all species of that which I say about its own cure.
In its own reduction, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is to the inner parts, then the patient should be laid on his back; and you should place the bandages over the root of the thigh in that place which is between the head of the thigh and the navel; and you should extend the bandage above the navel to the highest part of the breast.
And another minister should strongly take his arms and hold the large place of the patient's thigh, and extend it to the outer parts through violent extension, for that returns in its own place.
And this species is of the quickest cure by which that member is reduced, compared to the other species.
But if it is excused from you and does not obey the entrance with this species of cure at all, then it should be bound by both feet of the patient together with strong bandages, quickly, above on both heels and above on both soles.
And the length of each of his parts should be the quantity of four digits, and the chest of the patient should be extended more than the other by the quantity of two digits.
And the patient should be suspended over the top of the post, and it should be in the middle; and its length from the earth should be the quantity of two arms.
And command a strong young man to lessen the head of the thigh, and another young man should be suspended by the patient, and the other young man lessening the thigh with strength should push, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
But in the own reduction, when it is dislocated to the outer part, then the patient should lie on his back according to that which we have recommended in having gout; and the bandage should be tightened over the thigh of the patient properly and over his feet.
And two posts should be placed, one at his feet and the other at his head; and two posts adjusted to the middle of the mat which is placed over that upon which the patient is not damaged by the post being between his thighs, so that the extension to the lower parts is not drawn back.
And each minister should extend to his own part, and the doctor with his hand should draw out the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is to the front parts, then the chest should be extended slightly, and according to this measurement narration over mat; and the doctor places the palm of his right hand on the patient's navel, and another presses that with his other hand, and with that pressure he extends that to the lower parts to the navel.
If the dislocation is in the parts behind, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended to the lower parts and elevated above the ground; rather, let him be laid on a hard surface, just as if the joint is dislocated to the outside parts, according to what we have said about the placement of the patient on two surfaces. And he should be on his back with his limbs extended, as we have said before.
And let there be applied a compression with a board over the place where the joint is located.
Now that the reduction is complete, and if you see the sign of it, then you shall extend two limbs on each side of the patient; and when you see those parts become equal, and the patient's limb externalized and expanded for some excuse, then know that the member according to which he is returned. Then adjust both thighs and apply plaster and bind with a bandage so that no joint is injured in any part. And let the patient remain at rest for three or four days.
Then loosen the bandage and plaster, and compare one limb to the other.
Now, if you see those parts equal in appearance, then know that the dislocation is already closed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause for softening, then return and plaster that, and externalize it according to your first stretching, and let him remain in three days.
Then loosen that, and let him delay walking on any leg for some days, until he is comforted.
Chapter .xxxii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the jaw.
The jaw is dislocated in three ways: it is dislocated to the outside parts, and to the inner parts, and to the lower parts most to the back parts; but not to the front parts.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the patient to apply his limb to his thigh; then know that the jaw is dislocated.
And the restoration of all its ways is that you make the patient bend his head straight down, so that he extends his limb if he can on that; adhere your two hands to his face, and let him put his foot between your feet; then let someone else adhere his jaw with their palms, and let them hold those between their fingers over his jaw with strength, and another servant extend his foot while the jaw returns to its place.
And the sign of its reduction is that his limb adheres to the thigh with an easy non-laborious elevation.
Then plaster that and let the limb adhere to the thigh, and then tie those together with a bandage for three or four days, and then loosen them.
And do not use the patient of walking for some days, until he is healed and comforted.
But if the reduction of it to you is excused, then administer strong extension with bands of which in the cure of the hip previous narration was made.
Chapter .xxxiii.
Of the cure of the dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle sometimes is dislocated by small removal, and sometimes it is dislocated according to accomplishment; and its dislocation is made either to the inner parts or to the outer parts.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the ankle swollen proceeding to the part to which it is dislocated.
But the cure of its removal is by easy reduction, and it should be extended with ease with hands and equalized while returning.
But in its cure when it is dislocated according to accomplishment, let the patient bend his head straight down, and let there be one servant strong and valiant from the back part of that in the middle location of him.
Then you with your right hand hold the foot from the highest part, and with your left hand from the lowest part of the foot in the location of the ankle.
Then pull the foot to you with your right hand, and then with the left hand twice, and then extend with the left; and press the instep of the foot with the right towards the limb without labor.
And do that twice according to what we have counted.
Then press the instep of the foot to the limb the third time, and you are at the ankle.
Then if it returns in one time or two according to this manner, and if you see the foot equalized, it is good; but if not, then repeat the operation on him, for it will return.
But if to you is prohibited the reduction of it with that which we have commanded, then let the patient lie on his back on the ground, and stretch a rope tightly on the ground beneath him, and let there be a cushion between his thighs, and you have wrapped a cloth over him so that he does not kick.
Then the servant of him shall hold his thighs, and then another servant extend the foot with their hands or with band that lies on the neck of the foot; and then each servant shall extend against extension of his fellow, and the rope be extended between the thighs of the patient holding that so that the body of him is not drawn to the lower parts because of his extension.
Then the doctor equalizes the dislocation with their hands, and another servant shall hold the healthy thigh to the lower parts, for the dislocation returns with ease.
Then when the dislocation returns and you are made aware of the health of its reduction, then apply plaster and externalize that with strong bands, and bind the foot to the limb.
And if you fear or beware of the nerves which are behind on the ankle, so that the band over it is not violent and that does not lead him strongly.
Then let him remain for two days or three; and if the band is softened, externalize that.
Then loosen that on the third day or the fourth, and prohibit the patient of walking for 40 days. But if he is forced to walk before that space, there is no security that the dislocation is not dissolved and ruined, and then that he does not receive cure.
And if there happens to him pustule cautery, then let you administer in the healing of him that which in which our narration preceded, in another place of cure and of brooding, until it is healed.
Chapter .xxxiiii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the toes of the feet.
Let that which is dislocated be equalized with little extension without labor; and that which is difficult, then it is easier.
But if the dislocation is in some knuckles of the neck of the foot, then let the patient bend his foot on even ground or on a table, and be standing as if he was walking.
Then you stand on your feet, and put your foot on that which comes from the joints, and then press that with your foot with strength while it returns, and see that equalized and not prominent in the location.
Then rest the belly of his foot on a table that takes all his foot, at which are two caps, and then externalize that with decent and firm stretching for three days; then loosen that and let him rest from walking for many days, until he is stretched out and healed.
Chapter .xxxv.
Of the species of dislocation that are done by plague or fracture or by any wound.
When you encounter one of those causes, and you persevere in the cure of it and in the restoration of the patient, many times death occurs.
And for that reason one should not be hasty in the treatment of the patient, but rather he who is wise in the art, with great compassion and pity, being tardy, not rash or presumptuous; and one must use in the beginning medicines that produce suppuration only slightly, and let the patient go to divine judgement, unless it be to him from whom one hopes salvation from destruction, for the alleviation of the illness, and in which some hope appears. And therefore try at once to make the reduction of it at the beginning of the cause, before an abscess occurs.
And therefore if the limb returns as we desire, then administer treatment that will produce an abscess and cure the wound with those among the drying ointments that are appropriate for it.
And if the dislocation is due to a fracture and it happens that in the bones there are separated pieces, then you persevere in their setting, and do in that case what we have said in the simple illnesses of that which precedes in the places of those.
And guard and avoid according to your ability that of the instrument in the ways of fear as you have been instructed by my preaching; for what is better for you, you owe to your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and healthier for your true body those in your time. Thus end the words of Albucasim perfectly, of which may God be blessed. Amen.
